<tr>
<td colspan="4" style="line-height: 125%; font-size: 80%; padding: 2px 2px 2px 3px; border-top: 1px solid #777; background: #FEC; text-align:center;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_China" title="Template:History of China"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_China" title="Template talk:History of China"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_China&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Tang dynasty</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="/ŋ/: 'ng' in 'sing'">ŋ</span></span>/</a></span></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> <span class="IPA" lang="cmn-Latn-fonipa" style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Mandarin" title="Help:IPA/Mandarin">[tʰǎŋ]</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%94%90%E6%9C%9D" title="wikt:唐朝">唐朝</a></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[a]</a></sup>), or <b>Tang Empire</b>, was an <a href="/wiki/Dynasties_in_Chinese_history" title="Dynasties in Chinese history">imperial dynasty of China</a> that ruled from 618 to 907 AD, with an <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty_(690%E2%80%93705)" title="Zhou dynasty (690–705)">interregnum</a> between 690 and 705. It was preceded by the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a> and followed by the <a href="/wiki/Five_Dynasties_and_Ten_Kingdoms_period" title="Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms period">Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms period</a>. Historians generally regard the Tang as a high point in Chinese civilization, and a <a href="/wiki/Golden_age_(metaphor)" title="Golden age (metaphor)">golden age</a> of cosmopolitan culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis20121_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis20121-6">[5]</a></sup> Tang territory, acquired through the military campaigns of its early rulers, rivaled that of the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Li" title="House of Li">Lǐ family</a> (<span lang="zh">李</span>) founded the dynasty, seizing power during the decline and collapse of the Sui Empire and inaugurating a period of progress and stability in the first half of the dynasty's rule. The dynasty was formally interrupted during 690–705 when Empress <a href="/wiki/Wu_Zetian" title="Wu Zetian">Wu Zetian</a> seized the throne, proclaiming the <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty_(690%E2%80%93705)" title="Zhou dynasty (690–705)">Wu Zhou dynasty</a> and becoming the only legitimate Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_regnant" title="Empress regnant">empress regnant</a>. The devastating <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan_Rebellion" title="An Lushan Rebellion">An Lushan Rebellion</a> (755–763) shook the nation and led to the decline of central authority in the dynasty's latter half. Like the previous <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a>, the Tang maintained a civil-service system by recruiting <a href="/wiki/Scholar-official" title="Scholar-official">scholar-officials</a> through <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">standardized examinations</a> and recommendations to office. The rise of regional military governors known as <i><a href="/wiki/Jiedushi" title="Jiedushi">jiedushi</a></i> during the 9th century undermined this civil order. The dynasty and central government went into decline by the latter half of the 9th century; agrarian rebellions resulted in mass population loss and displacement, widespread poverty, and further government dysfunction that ultimately ended the dynasty in 907.
</p><p>The Tang capital at <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>) was then the world's most populous city. Two censuses of the 7th and 8th centuries estimated the empire's population at about 50 million people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999111,_141_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999111,_141-8">[7]</a></sup> which grew to an estimated 80 million by the dynasty's end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDu199837_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDu199837-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006106_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006106-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[b]</a></sup> From its numerous subjects, the dynasty raised professional and conscripted armies of hundreds of thousands of troops to contend with nomadic powers for <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty_in_Inner_Asia" title="Tang dynasty in Inner Asia">control</a> of <a href="/wiki/Inner_Asia" title="Inner Asia">Inner Asia</a> and the lucrative trade-routes along the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tributaries_of_Imperial_China" title="List of tributaries of Imperial China">Far-flung kingdoms and states paid tribute</a> to the Tang court, while the Tang also indirectly controlled several regions through a <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_(imperial_China)" title="Protectorate (imperial China)">protectorate</a> system. In addition to its political <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a>, the Tang exerted a powerful cultural influence over neighboring <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asian</a> nations such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Japan" title="Classical Japan">Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Korea</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chinese_culture" title="Chinese culture">Chinese culture</a> flourished and further matured during the Tang era. It is traditionally considered the greatest age for <a href="/wiki/Chinese_poetry" title="Chinese poetry">Chinese poetry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYu199873–87_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYu199873–87-12">[10]</a></sup> Two of China's most famous poets, <a href="/wiki/Li_Bai" title="Li Bai">Li Bai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Du_Fu" title="Du Fu">Du Fu</a>, belonged to this age, contributing with poets such as <a href="/wiki/Wang_Wei_(Tang_dynasty)" title="Wang Wei (Tang dynasty)">Wang Wei</a> to the monumental <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Hundred_Tang_Poems" title="Three Hundred Tang Poems">Three Hundred Tang Poems</a></i>. Many famous painters such as <a href="/wiki/Han_Gan" title="Han Gan">Han Gan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Xuan" title="Zhang Xuan">Zhang Xuan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Fang_(Tang_dynasty)" title="Zhou Fang (Tang dynasty)">Zhou Fang</a> were active, while Chinese court music flourished with instruments such as the popular <a href="/wiki/Pipa" title="Pipa">pipa</a>. Tang scholars compiled a rich variety of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_historiography" title="Chinese historiography">historical literature</a>, as well as encyclopedias and geographical works. Notable innovations included the development of <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">woodblock printing</a>. <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> became a major influence in Chinese culture, with <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism" title="Chinese Buddhism">native Chinese sects</a> gaining prominence. However, in the 840s, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wuzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Wuzong of Tang">Emperor Wuzong</a> enacted policies to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Anti-Buddhist_Persecution" title="Great Anti-Buddhist Persecution">suppress Buddhism</a>, which subsequently declined in influence.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Tang_dynasty" title="Timeline of the Tang dynasty">Timeline of the Tang dynasty</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment_and_early_reign">Establishment and early reign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Establishment and early reign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Transition_from_Sui_to_Tang" title="Transition from Sui to Tang">Transition from Sui to Tang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong of Tang</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TangGaozu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1983" data-file-width="929" decoding="async" height="363" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/TangGaozu.jpg/170px-TangGaozu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/TangGaozu.jpg/255px-TangGaozu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/TangGaozu.jpg/340px-TangGaozu.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TangGaozu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Portrait_painting" title="Portrait painting">Portrait painting</a> of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozu_of_Tang" title="Emperor Gaozu of Tang">Emperor Gaozu</a> (born Li Yuan, 566–635), the first Tang Emperor.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Li" title="House of Li">Li family</a> had ethnic <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han</a> origins, and it belonged to the northwest military aristocracy prevalent during the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200690–91_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200690–91-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200440–41_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200440–41-14">[12]</a></sup> According to official Tang records, they were <a href="/wiki/Patrilineality" title="Patrilineality">paternally descended</a> from the founder of <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lao_Tzu" title="Lao Tzu">Lao Tzu</a> (whose personal name was Li Dan or Li Er), the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> general <a href="/wiki/Li_Guang" title="Li Guang">Li Guang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Li_Gao" title="Li Gao">Li Gao</a>, the founder of the Han-ruled <a href="/wiki/Western_Liang_(Sixteen_Kingdoms)" title="Western Liang (Sixteen Kingdoms)">Western Liang</a> kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatourette1934191_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatourette1934191-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrompp2004126_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrompp2004126-16">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrompp2005376_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrompp2005376-17">[15]</a></sup> This family was known as the <a href="/wiki/Longxi_County" title="Longxi County">Longxi</a> Li lineage (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Longxi_Li_lineage&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Longxi Li lineage (page does not exist)">Li lineage</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E9%9A%B4%E8%A5%BF%E6%9D%8E%E6%B0%8F" title="zh:隴西李氏">zh</a>]</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">隴西李氏</span>), and it included the prominent Tang poet <a href="/wiki/Li_Bai" title="Li Bai">Li Bai</a>. Aside from traditional historiography, some modern historians have suggested that the Tang imperial family might have modified its genealogy to conceal <a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Xianbei</a> heritage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup> The Tang Emperors had part-<a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Xianbei</a> <a href="/wiki/Matrilineality" title="Matrilineality">maternal ancestry</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozu_of_Tang" title="Emperor Gaozu of Tang">Emperor Gaozu of Tang</a>'s part-Xianbei mother, <a href="/wiki/Dugu_sisters" title="Dugu sisters">Duchess Dugu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkaff2012125_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkaff2012125-20">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETogan2011177_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETogan2011177-21">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Da_Tang_in_seal_charcaters_on_the_epitaph_of_Shangguan_Wan%27er_(lid).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="944" data-file-width="502" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Da_Tang_in_seal_charcaters_on_the_epitaph_of_Shangguan_Wan%27er_%28lid%29.jpg/110px-Da_Tang_in_seal_charcaters_on_the_epitaph_of_Shangguan_Wan%27er_%28lid%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Da_Tang_in_seal_charcaters_on_the_epitaph_of_Shangguan_Wan%27er_%28lid%29.jpg/165px-Da_Tang_in_seal_charcaters_on_the_epitaph_of_Shangguan_Wan%27er_%28lid%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Da_Tang_in_seal_charcaters_on_the_epitaph_of_Shangguan_Wan%27er_%28lid%29.jpg/220px-Da_Tang_in_seal_charcaters_on_the_epitaph_of_Shangguan_Wan%27er_%28lid%29.jpg 2x" width="110"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Da_Tang_in_seal_charcaters_on_the_epitaph_of_Shangguan_Wan%27er_(lid).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Great Tang" (大唐 <i>Dà Táng</i>) in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seal_characters" title="Seal characters">seal characters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li_Yuan" title="Li Yuan">Li Yuan</a>, the founder of the Tang dynasty, was Duke of Tang and governor of <a href="/wiki/Taiyuan" title="Taiyuan">Taiyuan</a>, the capital of modern <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a>, during the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200690–91_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200690–91-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraff200078,_93_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraff200078,_93-23">[21]</a></sup> He had prestige and military experience, and was a first cousin of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Yang_of_Sui" title="Emperor Yang of Sui">Emperor Yang of Sui</a> (their mothers were both one of the <a href="/wiki/Dugu_sisters" title="Dugu sisters">Dugu sisters</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691-7">[6]</a></sup> Li Yuan rose in rebellion in 617, along with his son and his equally militant daughter <a href="/wiki/Princess_Pingyang" title="Princess Pingyang">Princess Pingyang</a> (d. 623), who raised and commanded her own troops. In winter 617, Li Yuan occupied <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a>, relegated Emperor Yang to the position of <i><a href="/wiki/Taishang_Huang" title="Taishang Huang">Taishang Huang</a></i> or retired emperor, and acted as <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regent</a> to the puppet child-emperor, <a href="/wiki/Yang_You" title="Yang You">Yang You</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200440_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200440-24">[22]</a></sup> On the news of Emperor Yang's murder by General <a href="/wiki/Yuwen_Huaji" title="Yuwen Huaji">Yuwen Huaji</a> on June 18, 618, Li Yuan declared himself the emperor of a new dynasty, the Tang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200440_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200440-24">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraff200078_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraff200078-25">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Li Yuan, known as <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozu_of_Tang" title="Emperor Gaozu of Tang">Emperor Gaozu of Tang</a>, ruled until 626, when he was forcefully deposed by his son <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Li Shimin</a>, the Prince of Qin.  Li Shimin had commanded troops since the age of 18 years old, had prowess with <a href="/wiki/Bow_and_arrow" title="Bow and arrow">bow and arrow</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_swords" title="Chinese swords">sword</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lance" title="Lance">lance</a> and was known for his effective <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> charges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraff200080_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraff200080-26">[24]</a></sup>  Fighting a numerically superior army, he defeated <a href="/wiki/Dou_Jiande" title="Dou Jiande">Dou Jiande</a> (573–621) at <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hulao" title="Battle of Hulao">Battle of Hulao</a> on May 28, 621.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200440–42_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200440–42-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraff200078,_82,_85–86,_95_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraff200078,_82,_85–86,_95-28">[26]</a></sup> In a violent elimination of royal family due to fear of assassination, Li Shimin ambushed and killed two of his brothers, <a href="/wiki/Li_Yuanji" title="Li Yuanji">Li Yuanji</a> (b. 603) and <a href="/wiki/Crown_prince" title="Crown prince">Crown prince</a> <a href="/wiki/Li_Jiancheng" title="Li Jiancheng">Li Jiancheng</a> (b. 589), in the <a href="/wiki/Xuanwu_Gate_Incident" title="Xuanwu Gate Incident">Xuanwu Gate Incident</a> on July 2, 626.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200442_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200442-29">[27]</a></sup>  Shortly thereafter, his father abdicated in his favor and Li Shimin ascended the throne. He is conventionally known by his <a href="/wiki/Temple_name" title="Temple name">temple name</a> Taizong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although killing two brothers and deposing his father contradicted the <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucian value</a> of <a href="/wiki/Filial_piety" title="Filial piety">filial piety</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200442_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200442-29">[27]</a></sup> Taizong showed himself to be a capable leader who listened to the advice of the wisest members of his council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691-7">[6]</a></sup> In 628, Emperor Taizong held a Buddhist memorial service for the casualties of war, and in 629 he had Buddhist monasteries erected at the sites of major battles so that monks could pray for the fallen on both sides of the fight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200693_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200693-30">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Tang_campaign_against_the_Eastern_Turks" title="Tang campaign against the Eastern Turks">Tang campaign against the Eastern Turks</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Turkic_Khaganate" title="Eastern Turkic Khaganate">Eastern Turkic Khaganate</a> was destroyed after the capture of its ruler, <a href="/wiki/Illig_Qaghan" title="Illig Qaghan">Illig Qaghan</a> by the famed Tang military officer <a href="/wiki/Li_Jing_(Tang_dynasty)" title="Li Jing (Tang dynasty)">Li Jing</a> (571–649), who later became a <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Tang_dynasty" title="Chancellor of the Tang dynasty">Chancellor of the Tang dynasty</a>. With this victory, the Turks accepted Taizong as their <a href="/wiki/Khagan" title="Khagan">khagan</a>, a title rendered as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tian_Kehan" title="Tian Kehan">Tian Kehan</a> in addition to his rule as <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">emperor of China</a> under the traditional title "<a href="/wiki/Son_of_Heaven" title="Son of Heaven">Son of Heaven</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200442–43_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200442–43-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000124_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000124-32">[30]</a></sup> Taizong was succeeded by his son Li Zhi (as <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Gaozong of Tang">Emperor Gaozong</a>) in 649 CE.
</p>

Tang dynasty, 7th century.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The Tang Dynasty further led the <a href="/wiki/Tang_campaigns_against_the_Western_Turks" title="Tang campaigns against the Western Turks">Tang campaigns against the Western Turks</a>. Early military conflicts were a result of the Tang interventions in the rivalry between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Turks" title="Western Turks">Western</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Turkic_Khaganate" title="Eastern Turkic Khaganate">Eastern Turks</a> in order to weaken both. Under <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong</a>, campaigns were dispatched in the Western Regions against <a href="/wiki/Gaochang" title="Gaochang">Gaochang</a> in 640, <a href="/wiki/Karasahr" title="Karasahr">Karasahr</a> in 644 and 648, and <a href="/wiki/Kucha" title="Kucha">Kucha</a> in 648. The wars against the Western Turks continued under <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Gaozong of Tang">Emperor Gaozong</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Western_Turkic_Khaganate" title="Western Turkic Khaganate">Western Turkic Khaganate</a> was finally annexed after General <a href="/wiki/Su_Dingfang" title="Su Dingfang">Su Dingfang</a>'s defeat of <a href="/wiki/Khagan" title="Khagan">Qaghan</a> <a href="/wiki/Ashina_Helu" title="Ashina Helu">Ashina Helu</a> in 657 CE.
</p><p>Around that time, the Tang court enjoyed the visit of numerous dignitaries from foreign lands. These were portraited in <i><a href="/wiki/Portraits_of_Periodical_Offering" title="Portraits of Periodical Offering">The Gathering of Kings</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">王會圖</span></span>, <i>Wanghuitu</i>), probably painted by <a href="/wiki/Yan_Liben" title="Yan Liben">Yan Liben</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">閻立本</span></span>, 601–673 CE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup> From right to left, the countries are <a href="/wiki/Lu_(state)" title="Lu (state)">Lu</a> (魯國) which is a reference to the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Wei" title="Eastern Wei">Eastern Wei</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rouran" title="Rouran">Rouran</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">芮芮國</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Persia</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">波斯國</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">百濟國</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Kunduz" title="Kunduz">Kumedh</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">胡密丹</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Baiti</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">白題國</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Mohe_people" title="Mohe people">Mohe people</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">靺國</span></span>), Central <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">中天竺</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">獅子國</span></span>), Northern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">北天竺</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Tashkurgan" title="Tashkurgan">Tashkurgan</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">謁盤陀</span></span>), Wuxing City of the <a href="/wiki/Chouchi" title="Chouchi">Chouchi</a> (武興國), <a href="/wiki/Kucha" title="Kucha">Kucha</a> (龜茲國), <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">倭國</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">高麗國</span></span>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khotan" title="Khotan">Khotan</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">于闐國</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">新羅國</span></span>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dangchang" title="Dangchang">Dangchang</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">宕昌國</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Langkasuka" title="Langkasuka">Langkasuka</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">狼牙修</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Qiang_(historical_people)" title="Qiang (historical people)">Dengzhi</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">鄧至國</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Yarkent_Khanate" title="Yarkent Khanate">Yarkand</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">周古柯</span></span>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kabadiyan" title="Kabadiyan">Kabadiyan</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">阿跋檀</span></span>), Barbarians of Jianping (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">建平蠻</span></span>), Nudan (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">女蜑國</span></span>).
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:1008px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E7%8E%8B%E4%BC%9A%E5%9B%BE_%E5%94%90%E9%98%8E%E7%AB%8B%E6%9C%AC_(annotations).jpg" title="Foreign ambassadors visiting the Tang court: The Gathering of Kings (王會圖), by Yan Liben (閻立本, 601–673 CE)."><img alt="" data-file-height="1558" data-file-width="11912" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/%E7%8E%8B%E4%BC%9A%E5%9B%BE_%E5%94%90%E9%98%8E%E7%AB%8B%E6%9C%AC_%28annotations%29.jpg/1000px-%E7%8E%8B%E4%BC%9A%E5%9B%BE_%E5%94%90%E9%98%8E%E7%AB%8B%E6%9C%AC_%28annotations%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/%E7%8E%8B%E4%BC%9A%E5%9B%BE_%E5%94%90%E9%98%8E%E7%AB%8B%E6%9C%AC_%28annotations%29.jpg/1500px-%E7%8E%8B%E4%BC%9A%E5%9B%BE_%E5%94%90%E9%98%8E%E7%AB%8B%E6%9C%AC_%28annotations%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/%E7%8E%8B%E4%BC%9A%E5%9B%BE_%E5%94%90%E9%98%8E%E7%AB%8B%E6%9C%AC_%28annotations%29.jpg/2000px-%E7%8E%8B%E4%BC%9A%E5%9B%BE_%E5%94%90%E9%98%8E%E7%AB%8B%E6%9C%AC_%28annotations%29.jpg 2x" width="1000"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%E7%8E%8B%E4%BC%9A%E5%9B%BE_%E5%94%90%E9%98%8E%E7%AB%8B%E6%9C%AC_(annotations).jpg" title="File:王会图 唐阎立本 (annotations).jpg"> </a></div>Foreign ambassadors visiting the Tang court: <i><a href="/wiki/Portraits_of_Periodical_Offering" title="Portraits of Periodical Offering">The Gathering of Kings</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">王會圖</span></span>), by Yan Liben (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">閻立本</span></span>, 601–673 CE).</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Wu_Zetian.27s_usurpation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wu_Zetian's_usurpation">Wu Zetian's usurpation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Wu Zetian's usurpation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty_(690%E2%80%93705)" title="Zhou dynasty (690–705)">Zhou dynasty (690–705)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_Tang_Dynasty_Empress_Wu_Zetian.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="393" data-file-width="347" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/A_Tang_Dynasty_Empress_Wu_Zetian.JPG/170px-A_Tang_Dynasty_Empress_Wu_Zetian.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/A_Tang_Dynasty_Empress_Wu_Zetian.JPG/255px-A_Tang_Dynasty_Empress_Wu_Zetian.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/A_Tang_Dynasty_Empress_Wu_Zetian.JPG/340px-A_Tang_Dynasty_Empress_Wu_Zetian.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Tang_Dynasty_Empress_Wu_Zetian.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Empress Wu (<a href="/wiki/Wu_Zetian" title="Wu Zetian">Wu Zetian</a>), the sole officially recognized empress regnant of China in more than two millennia. She first ruled through her husband and sons for almost three decades, then became emperor herself and ruled in her own right for another fifteen years.</div></div></div>
<p>Although she entered <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Gaozong of Tang">Emperor Gaozong</a>'s court as the lowly consort, <a href="/wiki/Wu_Zetian" title="Wu Zetian">Wu Zetian</a> rose to the highest seat of power in 690, establishing the short-lived Wu Zhou.  Empress Wu's rise to power was achieved through cruel and calculating tactics: a popular conspiracy theory stated that she killed her own baby girl and blamed it on Gaozong's empress so that the empress would be demoted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200697_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200697-34">[32]</a></sup>  Emperor Gaozong suffered a <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a> in 655, and Wu began to make many of his court decisions for him, discussing affairs of state with his councilors, who took orders from her while she sat behind a screen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200697–98_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200697–98-35">[33]</a></sup>  When Empress Wu's eldest son, the crown prince, began to assert his authority and advocate policies opposed by Empress Wu, he suddenly died in 675.  Many suspected he was poisoned by Empress Wu.  Although the next heir apparent kept a lower profile, in 680 he was accused by Wu of plotting a rebellion. He was then banished and later obliged to commit suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200698_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200698-36">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 683, Emperor Gaozong died. He was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Zhongzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Zhongzong of Tang">Emperor Zhongzong</a>, his eldest surviving son by Wu.  Zhongzong tried to appoint his wife's father as chancellor: after only six weeks on the throne, he was deposed by Empress Wu in favor of his younger brother, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Ruizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Ruizong of Tang">Emperor Ruizong</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200698_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200698-36">[34]</a></sup>  This provoked a group of Tang princes to rebel in 684.  Wu's armies suppressed them within two months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200698_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200698-36">[34]</a></sup>  She proclaimed the Tianshou <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_era_names" title="Chinese era names">era</a> of Wu Zhou on October 16, 690,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForte1988234_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForte1988234-37">[35]</a></sup> and three days later demoted Emperor Ruizong to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_prince_(China)" title="Crown prince (China)">crown prince</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarlowe200864_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarlowe200864-38">[36]</a></sup>  He was also forced to give up his father's surname Li in favor of the Empress Wu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarlowe200864_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarlowe200864-38">[36]</a></sup>  She then ruled as China's only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_regnant" title="Empress regnant">empress regnant</a>.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">palace coup</a> on February 20, 705, forced Empress Wu to yield her position on February 22.  The next day, her son Zhongzong was restored to power; the Tang was formally restored on March 3. She died soon after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200445_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200445-39">[37]</a></sup>  To legitimize her rule, she circulated a document known as the <i>Great Cloud Sutra</i>, which predicted that a <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Maitreya" title="Maitreya">Maitreya</a> Buddha would be a female monarch who would dispel illness, worry, and disaster from the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999116_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999116-40">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESen200397–98_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESen200397–98-41">[39]</a></sup>  She even introduced numerous revised <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters_of_Empress_Wu" title="Chinese characters of Empress Wu">written characters</a> to <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">the written language</a>, which reverted to the originals after her death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitfield200474_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitfield200474-42">[40]</a></sup>  Arguably the most important part of her legacy was diminishing the hegemony of the Northwestern aristocracy, allowing people from other clans and regions of China to become more represented in Chinese politics and government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200682_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200682-43">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer19858_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer19858-44">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Emperor_Xuanzong.27s_reign"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Emperor_Xuanzong's_reign">Emperor Xuanzong's reign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Emperor Xuanzong's reign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:312px;max-width:312px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:132px;max-width:132px"><div class="thumbimage" style="border:none;;height:173px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E8%A5%BF%E5%AE%89%E5%A4%A7%E9%9B%81%E5%A1%94.jpg"><img alt="西安大雁塔" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/%E8%A5%BF%E5%AE%89%E5%A4%A7%E9%9B%81%E5%A1%94.jpg/130px-%E8%A5%BF%E5%AE%89%E5%A4%A7%E9%9B%81%E5%A1%94.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/%E8%A5%BF%E5%AE%89%E5%A4%A7%E9%9B%81%E5%A1%94.jpg/195px-%E8%A5%BF%E5%AE%89%E5%A4%A7%E9%9B%81%E5%A1%94.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/%E8%A5%BF%E5%AE%89%E5%A4%A7%E9%9B%81%E5%A1%94.jpg/260px-%E8%A5%BF%E5%AE%89%E5%A4%A7%E9%9B%81%E5%A1%94.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Giant_Wild_Goose_Pagoda" title="Giant Wild Goose Pagoda">Giant Wild Goose Pagoda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a> (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>), built in 652, repaired by Empress Wu Zetian in 704.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:176px;max-width:176px"><div class="thumbimage" style="border:none;;height:173px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xi%27anwildgoosepagoda2.JPG"><img alt="Xi'anwildgoosepagoda2" data-file-height="544" data-file-width="544" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Xi%27anwildgoosepagoda2.JPG/174px-Xi%27anwildgoosepagoda2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Xi%27anwildgoosepagoda2.JPG/261px-Xi%27anwildgoosepagoda2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Xi%27anwildgoosepagoda2.JPG/348px-Xi%27anwildgoosepagoda2.JPG 2x" width="174"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Small_Wild_Goose_Pagoda" title="Small Wild Goose Pagoda">Small Wild Goose Pagoda</a>, built by 709, was adjacent to the Dajianfu Temple in Chang'an, where Buddhist monks gathered to translate <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> texts into Chinese<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKiang199912_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKiang199912-45">[43]</a></sup></div></div></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuanzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuanzong of Tang">Emperor Xuanzong of Tang</a></div>
<p>There were many prominent women at court during and after Wu's reign, including <a href="/wiki/Shangguan_Wan%27er" title="Shangguan Wan'er">Shangguan Wan'er</a> (664–710), a poet, writer, and trusted official in charge of Wu's private office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200446_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200446-46">[44]</a></sup> In 706 the wife of Emperor Zhongzong of Tang, <a href="/wiki/Empress_Wei_(Tang_dynasty)" title="Empress Wei (Tang dynasty)">Empress Wei</a> (d. 710), persuaded her husband to staff government offices with his sister and her daughters, and in 709 requested that he grant women the right to bequeath hereditary privileges to their sons (which before was a male right only).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn20026_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn20026-47">[45]</a></sup> Empress Wei eventually poisoned Zhongzong, whereupon she placed his fifteen-year-old son upon the throne in 710. Two weeks later, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuanzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuanzong of Tang">Li Longji</a> (the later Emperor Xuanzong) entered the palace with a few followers and slew Empress Wei and her faction. He then installed his father <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Ruizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Ruizong of Tang">Emperor Ruizong</a> (r. 710–712) on the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200699_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200699-48">[46]</a></sup> Just as Emperor Zhongzong was dominated by Empress Wei, so too was Ruizong dominated by <a href="/wiki/Princess_Taiping" title="Princess Taiping">Princess Taiping</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200447_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200447-49">[47]</a></sup> This was finally ended when Princess Taiping's coup failed in 712 (she later hanged herself in 713) and Emperor Ruizong abdicated to <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuanzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuanzong of Tang">Emperor Xuanzong</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200699_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200699-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn20026_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn20026-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 44-year reign of Emperor Xuanzong, the Tang dynasty reached its height, a golden age with low economic inflation and a toned down lifestyle for the imperial court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn20027_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn20027-50">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer19858_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer19858-44">[42]</a></sup> Seen as a progressive and benevolent ruler, Xuanzong even abolished the death penalty in the year 747; all executions had to be approved beforehand by the emperor himself (these were relatively few, considering that there were only 24 executions in the year 730).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200247_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200247-51">[49]</a></sup> Xuanzong bowed to the consensus of his ministers on policy decisions and made efforts to staff government ministries fairly with different political factions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200447_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200447-49">[47]</a></sup> His staunch Confucian chancellor <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Jiuling" title="Zhang Jiuling">Zhang Jiuling</a> (673–740) worked to reduce <a href="/wiki/Deflation" title="Deflation">deflation</a> and increase the money supply by upholding the use of private coinage, while his aristocratic and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technocracy_(bureaucratic)" title="Technocracy (bureaucratic)">technocratic</a> successor <a href="/wiki/Li_Linfu" title="Li Linfu">Li Linfu</a> (d. 753) favored government monopoly over the issuance of coinage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200489_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200489-52">[50]</a></sup> After 737, most of Xuanzong's confidence rested in his long-standing chancellor <a href="/wiki/Li_Linfu" title="Li Linfu">Li Linfu</a>, who championed a more aggressive foreign policy employing non-Chinese generals. This policy ultimately created the conditions for a massive rebellion against Xuanzong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200447–48_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200447–48-53">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="An_Lushan_Rebellion_and_catastrophe">An Lushan Rebellion and catastrophe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: An Lushan Rebellion and catastrophe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan_Rebellion" title="An Lushan Rebellion">An Lushan Rebellion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:An_Lushan_Rebellion.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="902" data-file-width="1275" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/An_Lushan_Rebellion.png/220px-An_Lushan_Rebellion.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/An_Lushan_Rebellion.png/330px-An_Lushan_Rebellion.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/An_Lushan_Rebellion.png/440px-An_Lushan_Rebellion.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:An_Lushan_Rebellion.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of An Lushan Rebellion</div></div></div>
<p>The Tang Empire was at its height of power up until the middle of the 8th century, when the <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan_Rebellion" title="An Lushan Rebellion">An Lushan Rebellion</a> (December 16, 755 – February 17, 763) destroyed the prosperity of the empire. <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan" title="An Lushan">An Lushan</a> was a half-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdian</a>, half-<a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turk</a> Tang commander since 744, who had experience fighting the <a href="/wiki/Khitan_people" title="Khitan people">Khitans</a> of <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> with a victory in 744,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100-54">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005184_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005184-55">[53]</a></sup> yet most of his campaigns against the Khitans were unsuccessful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEXu1993455–467_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEXu1993455–467-56">[54]</a></sup> He was given great responsibility in <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a>, which allowed him to rebel with an army of more than 100,000 troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100-54">[52]</a></sup> After capturing Luoyang, he named himself emperor of a new, but short-lived, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yan_(Anshi)" title="Yan (Anshi)">Yan state</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005184_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005184-55">[53]</a></sup> Despite early victories scored by Tang General <a href="/wiki/Guo_Ziyi" title="Guo Ziyi">Guo Ziyi</a> (697–781), the newly recruited troops of the army at the capital were no match for An Lushan's frontier veterans, so the court fled Chang'an.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100-54">[52]</a></sup> While the heir apparent raised troops in <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a> and Xuanzong fled to <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a> province, they called upon the help of the <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_Khaganate" title="Uyghur Khaganate">Uyghur Khaganate</a> in 756.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005185_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005185-57">[55]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uyghur_people" title="Uyghur people">Uyghur</a> khan <a href="/wiki/Bayanchur_Khan" title="Bayanchur Khan">Moyanchur</a> was greatly excited at this prospect, and married his own daughter to the Chinese diplomatic envoy once he arrived, receiving in turn a Chinese princess as his bride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005185_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005185-57">[55]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Uyghur_people" title="History of the Uyghur people">The Uyghurs</a> helped recapture the Tang capital from the rebels, but they refused to leave until the Tang paid them an enormous sum of tribute in silk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100-54">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005185_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005185-57">[55]</a></sup> Even <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasid_caliphate" title="Abbasid caliphate">Abbasid Arabs</a> assisted the Tang in putting down An Lushan's rebellion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005185_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005185-57">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer19859_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer19859-58">[56]</a></sup> A massacre of foreign Arab and Persian Muslim merchants by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tian_Shengong&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tian Shengong (page does not exist)">Tian Shengong</a> happened during the An Lushan rebellion in the <a href="/wiki/Yangzhou_massacre_(760)" title="Yangzhou massacre (760)">Yangzhou massacre (760)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWan201711_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWan201711-59">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQi2010221–227_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQi2010221–227-60">[58]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibetans</a> took hold of the opportunity and raided many areas under Chinese control, and even after the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Empire" title="Tibetan Empire">Tibetan Empire</a> had fallen apart in 842 (and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Qocho" title="Kingdom of Qocho">the Uyghurs soon after</a>) the Tang were in no position to <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_General_to_Pacify_the_West" title="Protectorate General to Pacify the West">reconquer Central Asia</a> after 763.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100-54">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESen200334_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESen200334-61">[59]</a></sup> So significant was this loss that half a century later <i>jinshi</i> examination candidates were required to write an essay on the causes of the Tang's decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGascoigneGascoigne200397_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGascoigneGascoigne200397-62">[60]</a></sup> Although An Lushan was killed by one of his eunuchs in 757,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005185_57-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005185-57">[55]</a></sup> this time of troubles and widespread insurrection continued until rebel <a href="/wiki/Shi_Siming" title="Shi Siming">Shi Siming</a> was killed by his own son in 763.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005185_57-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005185-57">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leshan_Buddha_Statue_View.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Leshan_Buddha_Statue_View.JPG/220px-Leshan_Buddha_Statue_View.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Leshan_Buddha_Statue_View.JPG/330px-Leshan_Buddha_Statue_View.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Leshan_Buddha_Statue_View.JPG/440px-Leshan_Buddha_Statue_View.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leshan_Buddha_Statue_View.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Leshan_Giant_Buddha" title="Leshan Giant Buddha">Leshan Giant Buddha</a>, 71 m (233 ft) high; begun in 713, completed in 803</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nanchan_Temple_1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Nanchan_Temple_1.JPG/220px-Nanchan_Temple_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Nanchan_Temple_1.JPG/330px-Nanchan_Temple_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Nanchan_Temple_1.JPG/440px-Nanchan_Temple_1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nanchan_Temple_1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanchan_Temple_(Wutai)" title="Nanchan Temple (Wutai)">Nanchan Temple (Wutai)</a>, built during the late 8th century</div></div></div>
<p>One of the legacies that the Tang government left since 710 was the gradual rise of regional military governors, the <a href="/wiki/Jiedushi" title="Jiedushi">jiedushi</a>, who slowly came to challenge the power of the central government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang200391_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang200391-63">[61]</a></sup> After the An Lushan Rebellion, the autonomous power and authority accumulated by <a href="/wiki/Three_Fanzhen_of_Hebei" title="Three Fanzhen of Hebei">the jiedushi in Hebei</a> went beyond the central government's control. After a series of rebellions between 781 and 784 in today's Hebei, <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a>, Hubei and <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a> provinces, the government had to officially acknowledge the jiedushi's hereditary ruling without accreditation. The Tang government relied on these governors and their armies for protection and to suppress locals that would take up arms against the government. In return, the central government would acknowledge the rights of these governors to maintain their army, collect taxes and even to pass on their title to heirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100_54-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100-54">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraff200843–44_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraff200843–44-64">[62]</a></sup> As time passed, these military governors slowly phased out the prominence of civil officials drafted by exams, and became more autonomous from central authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100_54-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100-54">[52]</a></sup> The rule of these powerful military governors lasted until 960, when a new civil order under the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> was established. Also, the abandonment of the equal-field system meant that people could buy and sell land freely. Many poor fell into debt because of this, forced to sell their land to the wealthy, which led to the exponential growth of large estates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100_54-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100-54">[52]</a></sup> With the breakdown of the land allocation system after 755, the central Chinese state barely interfered in agricultural management and acted merely as tax collector for roughly a millennium, save a few instances such as the Song's failed land nationalization during the 13th-century war with the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200490–91_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200490–91-65">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the central government collapsing in authority over the various regions of the empire, it was recorded in 845 that bandits and river pirates in parties of 100 or more began plundering settlements along the Yangtze River with little resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowman2000105_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowman2000105-66">[64]</a></sup> In 858, massive floods along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_of_China" title="Grand Canal of China">Grand Canal</a> inundated vast tracts of land and terrain of the <a href="/wiki/North_China_Plain" title="North China Plain">North China Plain</a>, which drowned tens of thousands of people in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowman2000105_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowman2000105-66">[64]</a></sup>
The Chinese belief in the <a href="/wiki/Mandate_of_Heaven" title="Mandate of Heaven">Mandate of Heaven</a> granted to the ailing Tang was also challenged when natural calamities occurred, forcing many to believe that the Tang had lost their right to rule. Furthermore, in 873 a disastrous harvest shook the foundations of the empire; in some areas only half of all agricultural produce was gathered, and tens of thousands faced famine and starvation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowman2000105_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowman2000105-66">[64]</a></sup> In the earlier period of the Tang, the central government was able to meet crises in the harvest, as it was recorded from 714 to 719 that the Tang government responded effectively to natural disasters by extending the price-regulation <a href="/wiki/Granary" title="Granary">granary</a> system throughout the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowman2000105_66-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowman2000105-66">[64]</a></sup> The central government was able then to build a large surplus stock of foods to ward off the rising danger of famine and increased agricultural productivity through <a href="/wiki/Land_reclamation" title="Land reclamation">land reclamation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn20027_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn20027-50">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowman2000105_66-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowman2000105-66">[64]</a></sup> In the 9th century, however, the Tang government was nearly helpless in dealing with any calamity.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rebuilding_and_recovery">Rebuilding and recovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Rebuilding and recovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xumipagodazhengding.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Xumipagodazhengding.jpg/170px-Xumipagodazhengding.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Xumipagodazhengding.jpg/255px-Xumipagodazhengding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Xumipagodazhengding.jpg/340px-Xumipagodazhengding.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xumipagodazhengding.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Xumi_Pagoda" title="Xumi Pagoda">Xumi Pagoda</a>, built in 636</div></div></div>
<p>Although these natural calamities and rebellions stained the reputation and hampered the effectiveness of the central government, the early 9th century is nonetheless viewed as a period of recovery for the Tang dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200215–17_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200215–17-67">[65]</a></sup> The government's withdrawal from its role in managing the economy had the unintended effect of stimulating trade, as more markets with fewer bureaucratic restrictions were opened up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006101_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006101-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200685_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200685-69">[67]</a></sup> By 780, the old grain tax and labor service of the 7th century was replaced by a semiannual tax paid in cash, signifying the shift to a money economy boosted by the merchant class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer19859_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer19859-58">[56]</a></sup> Cities in the <a href="/wiki/Jiangnan" title="Jiangnan">Jiangnan</a> region to the south, such as <a href="/wiki/Yangzhou" title="Yangzhou">Yangzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suzhou" title="Suzhou">Suzhou</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a> prospered the most economically during the late Tang period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006101_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006101-68">[66]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Salt_in_Chinese_history" title="Salt in Chinese history">government monopoly on the production of salt</a>, weakened after the <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan_Rebellion" title="An Lushan Rebellion">An Lushan Rebellion</a>, was placed under the <a href="/wiki/Salt_Commission" title="Salt Commission">Salt Commission</a>, which became one of the most powerful state agencies, run by capable ministers chosen as specialists. The commission began the practice of selling merchants the rights to buy monopoly salt, which they would then transport and sell in local markets. In 799 salt accounted for over half of the government's revenues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100_54-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100-54">[52]</a></sup> S.A.M. Adshead writes that this salt tax represents "the first time that an indirect tax, rather than tribute, levies on land or people, or profit from state enterprises such as mines, had been the primary resource of a major state."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200450_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200450-70">[68]</a></sup> Even after the power of the central government was in decline after the mid 8th century, it was still able to function and give out imperial orders on a massive scale. The <i>Tangshu</i> (<a href="/wiki/Old_Book_of_Tang" title="Old Book of Tang">Old Book of Tang</a>) compiled in the year 945 recorded that in 828 the Tang government issued a decree that standardized <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigational</a> square-pallet <a href="/wiki/Chain_pump" title="Chain pump">chain pumps</a> in the country:
</p>
<blockquote><p>In the second year of the Taihe reign period [828], in the second month<span class="nowrap"> </span>... a standard model of the chain pump was issued from the palace, and the people of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jingzhao_Fu" title="Jingzhao Fu">Jingzhao Fu</a> (d footnote: the capital) were ordered by the emperor to make a considerable number of machines, for distribution to the people along the <a href="/wiki/Zhengguo_Canal" title="Zhengguo Canal">Zheng Bai Canal</a>, for irrigation purposes.|<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b347_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b347-71">[69]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The last great ambitious ruler of the Tang dynasty was <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xianzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xianzong of Tang">Emperor Xianzong</a> (r. 805–820), whose reign was aided by the fiscal reforms of the 780s, including a government monopoly on the salt industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200214–15_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200214–15-72">[70]</a></sup> He also had an effective and well-trained imperial army stationed at the capital led by his court eunuchs; this was the Army of Divine Strategy, numbering 240,000 in strength as recorded in 798.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200215_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200215-73">[71]</a></sup> Between the years 806 and 819, Emperor Xianzong conducted seven major military campaigns to quell the rebellious provinces that had claimed autonomy from central authority, managing to subdue all but two of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200451_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200451-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200216_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200216-75">[73]</a></sup> Under his reign there was a brief end to the hereditary jiedushi, as Xianzong appointed his own military officers and staffed the regional bureaucracies once again with civil officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200451_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200451-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200216_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200216-75">[73]</a></sup> However, Xianzong's successors proved less capable and more interested in the leisure of hunting, feasting, and playing outdoor sports, allowing eunuchs to amass more power as drafted scholar-officials caused strife in the bureaucracy with factional parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200216_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200216-75">[73]</a></sup> The eunuchs' power became unchallenged after <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wenzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Wenzong of Tang">Emperor Wenzong</a>'s (r. 826–840) failed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganlu_Incident" title="Ganlu Incident">plot to have them overthrown</a>; instead the allies of Emperor Wenzong were publicly executed in the <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an#West_Central_Chang'an" title="Chang'an">West Market of Chang'an</a>, by the eunuchs' command.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006101_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006101-68">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dunhuang_Zhang_Yichao_army.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Dunhuang_Zhang_Yichao_army.jpg/400px-Dunhuang_Zhang_Yichao_army.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Dunhuang_Zhang_Yichao_army.jpg/600px-Dunhuang_Zhang_Yichao_army.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Dunhuang_Zhang_Yichao_army.jpg/800px-Dunhuang_Zhang_Yichao_army.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dunhuang_Zhang_Yichao_army.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A late Tang mural commemorating the victory of General <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Yichao" title="Zhang Yichao">Zhang Yichao</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Empire" title="Tibetan Empire">Tibetans</a> in 848 AD, from <a href="/wiki/Mogao_Caves" title="Mogao Caves">Mogao cave</a> 156</div></div></div>
<p>However, the Tang did manage to restore at least indirect control over former Tang territories as far west as the <a href="/wiki/Hexi_Corridor" title="Hexi Corridor">Hexi Corridor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang" title="Dunhuang">Dunhuang</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a>. In 848 the ethnic Han Chinese general <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Yichao" title="Zhang Yichao">Zhang Yichao</a> (799–872) managed to wrestle control of the region from the <a href="/wiki/Era_of_Fragmentation" title="Era of Fragmentation">Tibetan Empire during its civil war</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaenzer201635–37_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaenzer201635–37-76">[74]</a></sup> Shortly afterwards <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Xu%C4%81nzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuānzong of Tang">Emperor Xuānzong of Tang</a> (r. 846–859) acknowledged Zhang as the protector (防禦使, <i>Fangyushi</i>) of Sha Prefecture and <i>jiedushi</i> military governor of the new <a href="/wiki/Guiyi_Circuit" title="Guiyi Circuit">Guiyi Circuit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZZTJ249_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZZTJ249-77">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Tang dynasty recovered its power decades after the An Lushan rebellion and was still able to launch offensive conquests and campaigns like its <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_Khaganate#Fall" title="Uyghur Khaganate">destruction of the Uyghur Khaganate in Mongolia in 840–847</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaumer2012310_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaumer2012310-78">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_the_dynasty">End of the dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: End of the dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In addition to natural calamities and jiedushi amassing autonomous control, the <a href="/wiki/Huang_Chao" title="Huang Chao">Huang Chao Rebellion</a> (874–884) resulted in the sacking of both Chang'an and Luoyang, and took an entire decade to suppress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005189–190_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005189–190-79">[77]</a></sup> It was the Huang Chao rebellion by the native Han rebel Huang Chao that permanently destroyed the power of the Tang dynasty since Huang Chao not only devastated the north but marched into southern China which An Lushan failed to do due to the Battle of Suiyang. Huang Chao's army in southern China committed the <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou_massacre" title="Guangzhou massacre">Guangzhou massacre</a> against foreign Arab and Persian Muslim, Zoroastrian, Jewish and Christian merchants in 878–879 at the seaport and trading port of <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1996292_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1996292-80">[78]</a></sup> and captured both Tang dynasty capitals, Luoyang and Chang'an. A medieval Chinese source claimed that Huang Chao killed 8 million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup> The Tang never recovered from this rebellion, weakening it for future military powers to replace it. Large groups of bandits in the size of small armies ravaged the countryside in the last years of the Tang. They smuggled illicit salt, ambushed merchants and convoys, and even besieged several walled cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006108_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006108-82">[80]</a></sup> Amid the sacking of cities and murderous factional strife among eunuchs and officials, the top tier of aristocratic families, which had amassed a large fraction of the landed wealth and official positions, was largely destroyed or marginalized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMote20036–7_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMote20036–7-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the last two decades of the Tang dynasty, the gradual collapse of central authority led to the rise of two prominent rival military figures over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_China" title="Northern China">northern China</a>: <a href="/wiki/Li_Keyong" title="Li Keyong">Li Keyong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Wen" title="Zhu Wen">Zhu Wen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMote20037–12_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMote20037–12-85">[83]</a></sup> Tang forces had defeated Huang Chao's rebellion with the crucial aid of allied <a href="/wiki/Shatuo" title="Shatuo">Shatuo</a> <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic peoples</a> of what is now <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a> led by Li Keyong. He was made a jiedushi governor and later <a href="/wiki/Jin_(Later_Tang_precursor)" title="Jin (Later Tang precursor)">Prince of Jin</a>, bestowed with the imperial surname Li by the Tang court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMote20036–7,_10,_12_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMote20036–7,_10,_12-86">[84]</a></sup> Zhu Wen, originally a salt smuggler who served as a lieutenant under the rebel Huang Chao, surrendered to Tang forces. By helping to defeat Huang, he was <a href="/wiki/Courtesy_name" title="Courtesy name">renamed</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhu_Quanzhong" title="Zhu Quanzhong">Zhu Quanzhong</a> ("Zhu of Perfect Loyalty") and granted a series of rapid military promotions to military governor of Xuanwu Circuit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMote20037,_10,_12_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMote20037,_10,_12-87">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c320–321,_footnote_h_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c320–321,_footnote_h-88">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 901, from his powerbase of <a href="/wiki/Kaifeng" title="Kaifeng">Kaifeng</a>, Zhu Wen seized control of the Tang capital Chang'an and with it the imperial family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMote20037_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMote20037-89">[87]</a></sup> By 903 he forced <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Zhaozong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Zhaozong of Tang">Emperor Zhaozong of Tang</a> to move the capital to Luoyang, preparing to take the throne for himself. In 904 Zhu assassinated Emperor Zhaozong to replace him with the emperor's young son <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Ai_of_Tang" title="Emperor Ai of Tang">Emperor Ai of Tang</a>. In 905 Zhu executed the brothers of Emperor Ai as well as many officials and <a href="/wiki/Empress_He_(Tang_dynasty)" title="Empress He (Tang dynasty)">Empress Dowager He</a>. In 907 the Tang dynasty was ended when Zhu deposed Ai and took the throne for himself (known posthumously as Emperor Taizu of Later Liang). He established the <a href="/wiki/Later_Liang_(Five_Dynasties)" title="Later Liang (Five Dynasties)">Later Liang</a>, which inaugurated the <a href="/wiki/Five_Dynasties_and_Ten_Kingdoms_period" title="Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms period">Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms period</a>. A year later Zhu had the deposed Emperor Ai poisoned to death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMote20037,_10,_12_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMote20037,_10,_12-87">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zhu Wen's hated nemesis Li Keyong died in 908 but, out of loyalty to Tang, Li never claimed the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_sovereign" title="Chinese sovereign">title of emperor</a>. His son <a href="/wiki/Li_Cunxu" title="Li Cunxu">Li Cunxu</a> (Emperor Zhuangzong) inherited his title Prince of Jin along with his father's rivalry against Zhu. In 923 Li Cunxu declared a "restored" Tang dynasty, the <a href="/wiki/Later_Tang" title="Later Tang">Later Tang</a>, before toppling the Later Liang dynasty the same year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMote200310,_12–13_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMote200310,_12–13-90">[88]</a></sup> However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_China" title="Southern China">southern China</a> would remain splintered into various small kingdoms until most of China was reunified under the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> (960–1279).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMote200310–11_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMote200310–11-91">[89]</a></sup> Control over parts of <a href="/wiki/Northeast_China" title="Northeast China">northeast China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Liao_dynasty" title="Liao dynasty">Liao dynasty</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Khitan_people" title="Khitan people">Khitan people</a> also stemmed from this period. In 905 their leader <a href="/wiki/Abaoji" title="Abaoji">Abaoji</a> formed a military alliance with Li Keyong against Zhu Wen but the Khitans eventually turned against the Later Tang, helping another Shatuo leader <a href="/wiki/Shi_Jingtang" title="Shi Jingtang">Shi Jingtang</a> of <a href="/wiki/Later_Jin_(Five_Dynasties)" title="Later Jin (Five Dynasties)">Later Jin</a> to overthrow Later Tang in 936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMote200312–13_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMote200312–13-92">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_politics">Administration and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Administration and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_the_Tang_dynasty" title="Administrative divisions of the Tang dynasty">Administrative divisions of the Tang dynasty</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_reforms">Initial reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Initial reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tang_XianZong.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="343" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Tang_XianZong.jpg/170px-Tang_XianZong.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Tang_XianZong.jpg/255px-Tang_XianZong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Tang_XianZong.jpg/340px-Tang_XianZong.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tang_XianZong.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuanzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuanzong of Tang">Emperor Xuanzong of Tang</a> wearing the robes and hat of a scholar</div></div></div>
<p>Taizong set out to solve internal problems within the government which had constantly plagued past dynasties.
