Building upon the Sui <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Code_(law)" title="Code (law)">legal code</a>, he issued <a href="/wiki/Tang_Code" title="Tang Code">a new legal code</a> that subsequent Chinese dynasties would model theirs upon, as well as neighboring polities in <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691-7">[6]</a></sup> The earliest law code to survive was the one established in the year 653, which was divided into 500 articles specifying different crimes and penalties ranging from ten blows with a light stick, one hundred blows with a heavy rod, exile, penal servitude, or execution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999111–112_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999111–112-93">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>The legal code distinguished different levels of severity in meted punishments when different members of the social and political hierarchy committed the same crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999112_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999112-94">[92]</a></sup> For example, the severity of punishment was different when a servant or nephew killed a master or an uncle than when a master or uncle killed a servant or nephew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999112_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999112-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_tri-coloured_figure_of_a_civil_official.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_tri-coloured_figure_of_a_civil_official.jpg/170px-CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_tri-coloured_figure_of_a_civil_official.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_tri-coloured_figure_of_a_civil_official.jpg/255px-CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_tri-coloured_figure_of_a_civil_official.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_tri-coloured_figure_of_a_civil_official.jpg/340px-CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_tri-coloured_figure_of_a_civil_official.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_tri-coloured_figure_of_a_civil_official.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tang <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty_tomb_figures" title="Tang dynasty tomb figures">tomb figure</a> of an official dressed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Chinese_clothing" title="Han Chinese clothing">Hanfu</a>, with a tall hat, wide-sleeved belted outer garment, and rectangular "kerchief" in front. A white inner gown hangs over his square shoes. He holds a tablet to his chest, a report to his superiors.</div></div></div>
<p>The Tang Code was largely retained by later codes such as the early <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> (1368–1644) code of 1397,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrewRapp200025_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrewRapp200025-95">[93]</a></sup> yet there were several revisions in later times, such as <a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_Song_dynasty#Women:_legality_and_lifestyles" title="Society of the Song dynasty">improved property rights for women</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> (960–1279).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999158_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999158-96">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBernhardt1995274–275_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBernhardt1995274–275-97">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Tang had three departments (Chinese: <span lang="zh-Hant">省</span>; pinyin: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">shěng</span></i>), which were obliged to draft, review, and implement policies respectively. There were also six ministries (Chinese: <span lang="zh-Hant">部</span>; pinyin: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">bù</span></i>) under the administrations that implemented policy, each of which was assigned different tasks. These <a href="/wiki/Three_Departments_and_Six_Ministries" title="Three Departments and Six Ministries">Three Departments and Six Ministries</a> included the personnel administration, finance, rites, military, justice, and public works—an administrative model which would last until the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> (1644–1912).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200678_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200678-98">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the founders of the Tang related to the glory of the earlier <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> (3rd century BC–3rd century AD), the basis for much of their administrative organization was very similar to the previous <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">Northern and Southern dynasties</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691-7">[6]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Northern_Zhou" title="Northern Zhou">Northern Zhou</a> (6th century) <a href="/wiki/Fubing_system" title="Fubing system">fubing system</a> of divisional militia was continued by the Tang, along with farmer-soldiers serving in rotation from the capital or frontier in order to receive appropriated farmland. The <a href="/wiki/Equal-field_system" title="Equal-field system">equal-field system</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Wei" title="Northern Wei">Northern Wei</a> (4th–6th centuries) was also kept, although there were a few modifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the central and local governments kept an enormous number of records about land property in order to assess taxes, it became common practice in the Tang for literate and affluent people to create their own private documents and signed contracts. These had their own signature and that of a witness and scribe in order to prove in court (if necessary) that their claim to property was legitimate. The prototype of this actually existed since the ancient Han dynasty, while contractual language became even more common and embedded into Chinese literary culture in later dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199859_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199859-99">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>The center of the political power of the Tang was the capital city of <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>), where the emperor maintained his large palace quarters and entertained political emissaries with music, sports, <a href="/wiki/Acrobatics" title="Acrobatics">acrobatic</a> stunts, poetry, paintings, and <a href="/wiki/Pear_Garden" title="Pear Garden">dramatic theater performances</a>. The capital was also filled with incredible amounts of riches and resources to spare. When the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Prefecture" title="Prefecture">prefectural</a> government officials traveled to the capital in the year 643 to give the annual report of the affairs in their districts, Emperor Taizong discovered that many had no proper quarters to rest in and were renting rooms with merchants. Therefore, Emperor Taizong ordered the government agencies in charge of <a href="/wiki/Township" title="Township">municipal</a> construction to build every visiting official his own private <a href="/wiki/Mansion" title="Mansion">mansion</a> in the capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200259_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200259-100">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Imperial_examinations">Imperial examinations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Imperial examinations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">Imperial examination</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination_in_Chinese_mythology" title="Imperial examination in Chinese mythology">Imperial examination in Chinese mythology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Civilserviceexam1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="427" data-file-width="572" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Civilserviceexam1.jpg/220px-Civilserviceexam1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Civilserviceexam1.jpg/330px-Civilserviceexam1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Civilserviceexam1.jpg/440px-Civilserviceexam1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Civilserviceexam1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Civil service exam candidates gather around the wall where results had been posted. Artwork by <a href="/wiki/Qiu_Ying" title="Qiu Ying">Qiu Ying</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Students of <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucian</a> studies were candidates for the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">imperial examinations</a>, which qualified their graduates for appointment to the local, provincial, and central government bureaucracies. Two types of exams given, <i>mingjing</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">明經</span></span>; "illuminating the classics") and <i>jinshi</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">進士</span></span>; "presented scholar").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691–92_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691–92-101">[99]</a></sup> The <i>mingjing</i> was based upon the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_classic_texts" title="Chinese classic texts">Confucian classics</a> and tested the student's knowledge of a broad variety of texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691–92_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691–92-101">[99]</a></sup> The <i>jinshi</i> tested a student's literary abilities in writing essays in response to questions on governance and politics, as well as in composing <a href="/wiki/Chinese_poetry" title="Chinese poetry">poetry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200692_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200692-102">[100]</a></sup> Candidates were also judged on proper deportment, appearance, speech, and <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a>, all subjective criteria that favored the wealthy over those of more modest means who were unable to pay tutors of <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a> and writing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200697_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200697-34">[32]</a></sup> Although a disproportionate number of civil officials came from aristocratic families,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200697_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200697-34">[32]</a></sup> wealth and noble status were not prerequisites, and the exams were open to all male subjects whose fathers were not of the <a href="/wiki/Four_occupations" title="Four occupations">artisan or merchant classes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGascoigneGascoigne200395_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGascoigneGascoigne200395-103">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200697_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200697-34">[32]</a></sup> To promote widespread Confucian education, the Tang government established state-run schools and issued standard versions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Classics" title="Five Classics">Five Classics</a> with commentaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999112_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999112-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Open competition was designed to draw the best talent into government. But perhaps an even greater consideration for the Tang rulers was to avoid imperial dependence on powerful aristocratic families and <a href="/wiki/Warlord" title="Warlord">warlords</a> by recruiting a body of career officials having no family or local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Base_(politics)" title="Base (politics)">power base</a>. The Tang law code ensured equal division of inherited property amongst legitimate heirs, encouraging <a href="/wiki/Social_mobility" title="Social mobility">social mobility</a> by preventing powerful families from becoming <a href="/wiki/Landed_nobility" title="Landed nobility">landed nobility</a> through <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">primogeniture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200683_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200683-104">[102]</a></sup> The competition system proved successful, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-bureaucrats" title="Scholar-bureaucrats">scholar-officials</a> acquired status in their local communities while developing an <a href="/wiki/Morale" title="Morale">esprit de corps</a> that connected them to the imperial court. From Tang times until the end of the Qing dynasty in 1912, scholar-officials served as intermediaries between the <a href="/wiki/Grassroots" title="Grassroots">people</a> and the government.
</p><p>Yet the potential of a widespread examination system was not fully realized until the succeeding Song dynasty, when the merit-driven scholar official largely shed his aristocratic habits and defined his social status through the examination system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159-105">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200695_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200695-106">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200454_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200454-107">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The examination system, used only on a small scale in Sui and Tang times, played a central role in the fashioning of this new elite. The early Song emperors, concerned above all to avoid domination of the government by military men, greatly expanded the civil service examination system and the government school system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999145–146_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999145–146-108">[106]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion_and_politics">Religion and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Religion and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Audience_by_Emperor_Tang_Xuanzong.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="1063" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Audience_by_Emperor_Tang_Xuanzong.jpg/250px-Audience_by_Emperor_Tang_Xuanzong.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Audience_by_Emperor_Tang_Xuanzong.jpg/375px-Audience_by_Emperor_Tang_Xuanzong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Audience_by_Emperor_Tang_Xuanzong.jpg/500px-Audience_by_Emperor_Tang_Xuanzong.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Audience_by_Emperor_Tang_Xuanzong.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuanzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuanzong of Tang">Emperor Xuanzong of Tang</a> giving audience to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elder_Zhang_Guo" title="Elder Zhang Guo">Zhang Guo</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Ren_Renfa" title="Ren Renfa">Ren Renfa</a> (1254–1327)</div></div></div>
<p>From the outset, religion played a role in Tang politics. In his bid for power, Li Yuan had attracted a following by claiming descent from the <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> sage <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lao_Tzu" title="Lao Tzu">Lao Tzu</a> (<abbr title="floruit ('flourished' – known to have been active at a particular time or during a particular period)">fl.</abbr> 6th century BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraff200079_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraff200079-109">[107]</a></sup> People bidding for office would request the prayers of Buddhist monks, with successful aspirants making donations in return. Before the persecution of Buddhism in the 9th century, Buddhism and Taoism were both accepted.
</p><p>Religion was central in the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuanzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuanzong of Tang">Emperor Xuanzong</a> (r. 712–756). The Emperor invited Taoist and Buddhist monks and clerics to his court, exalted the Taoist ancient Lao Tzu with grand titles, wrote commentary on the <i>Lao Tzu</i> scriptures, and set up a school to prepare candidates for Taoist examinations. In 726 he called upon the Indian monk <a href="/wiki/Vajrabodhi" title="Vajrabodhi">Vajrabodhi</a> (671–741) to perform <a href="/wiki/Vajrayana" title="Vajrayana">Tantric</a> rites to avert a drought. In 742 he personally held the incense burner while <a href="/wiki/Amoghavajra" title="Amoghavajra">Amoghavajra</a> (705–774, patriarch of the <a href="/wiki/Shingon_Buddhism" title="Shingon Buddhism">Shingon school</a>) recited "mystical incantations to secure the victory of Tang forces."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200699_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200699-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Emperor Xuanzong closely regulated religious finances. Near the beginning of his reign in 713, he liquidated the <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an#Northwestern_Chang'an" title="Chang'an">Inexhaustible Treasury</a> of a prominent Buddhist monastery in Chang'an which had collected vast riches as multitudes of anonymous repentants left money, silk, and treasure at its doors. Although the monastery used its funds generously, the Emperor condemned it for fraudulent <a href="/wiki/History_of_banking_in_China" title="History of banking in China">banking</a> practices, and distributed its wealth to other Buddhist and Taoist monasteries, and to repair local statues, halls, and bridges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200261_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200261-110">[108]</a></sup> In 714, he forbade Chang'an shops from selling copied Buddhist sutras, giving a monopoly of this trade to the Buddhist clergy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200257_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200257-111">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxes_and_the_census">Taxes and the census</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Taxes and the census">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Tang dynasty government attempted to create an accurate census of the empire's population, mostly for effective taxation and military conscription. The early Tang government established modest grain and cloth taxes on each household, persuading households to register and provide the government with accurate demographic information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691-7">[6]</a></sup> In the official census of 609, the population was tallied at 9 million households, about 50 million people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691_7-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691-7">[6]</a></sup> and this number did not increase in the census of 742.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999141_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999141-112">[110]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patricia_Ebrey" title="Patricia Ebrey">Patricia Ebrey</a> writes that nonwithstanding census undercounting, China's population had not grown significantly since the earlier <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">Han Dynasty</a>, which recorded 58 million people in the year 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691_7-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986595–596_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986595–596-113">[111]</a></sup> S.A.M. Adshead disagrees, estimating about 75 million people by 750.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200472_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200472-114">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Tang census of 754, there were 1,859 cities, 321 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prefecture_(China)" title="Prefecture (China)">prefectures</a>, and 1,538 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counties_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China#History" title="Counties of the People's Republic of China">counties</a> throughout the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200245_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200245-115">[113]</a></sup> Although there were many large and prominent cities, the rural and agrarian areas comprised some 80 to 90% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200232_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200232-116">[114]</a></sup> There was also a dramatic migration from <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_southern_China" title="Northern and southern China">northern to southern China</a>, as the North held 75% of the overall population at the dynasty's inception, which by its end was reduced to 50%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200475_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200475-117">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chinese population would not dramatically increase until the Song dynasty, when it doubled to 100 million because of extensive rice cultivation in central and southern China, coupled with higher yields of grain sold in a growing market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006156_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006156-118">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_and_foreign_policy">Military and foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Military and foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_the_Sui%E2%80%93Tang_dynasties" title="Military history of the Sui–Tang dynasties">Military history of the Sui–Tang dynasties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naval_history_of_China" title="Naval history of China">Naval history of China</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jimi_system" title="Jimi system">Jimi system</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Guards_(Tang_dynasty)" title="Imperial Guards (Tang dynasty)">Imperial Guards (Tang dynasty)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1049" data-file-width="1890" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg/300px-Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg/450px-Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg/600px-Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Taizong_gives_an_audience_to_the_ambassador_of_Tibet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong</a> (r. 626–649) receives <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gar_Tongtsen_Y%C3%BClsung" title="Gar Tongtsen Yülsung">Gar Tongtsen Yülsung</a>, ambassador of the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Empire" title="Tibetan Empire">Tibetan Empire</a>, at his court; <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_Receiving_the_Tibetan_Envoy" title="Emperor Taizong Receiving the Tibetan Envoy">later copy</a> of an original painted in 641 by <a href="/wiki/Yan_Liben" title="Yan Liben">Yan Liben</a> (600–673)</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protectorates_and_tributaries">Protectorates and tributaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Protectorates and tributaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The 7th and first half of the 8th century are generally considered to be the era in which the Tang reached the zenith of its power. In this period, Tang control extended further west than any previous dynasty, stretching from north Vietnam in the south, to a point north of <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a> bordering Persia in the west, to northern Korea in the north-east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002xii,_4_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002xii,_4-119">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the kingdoms paying tribute to the Tang dynasty included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kashmir_region" title="Kashmir region">Kashmir</a>, Nepal, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khotan" title="Khotan">Khotan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kucha" title="Kucha">Kucha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kashgar" title="Kashgar">Kashgar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Champa" title="Champa">Champa</a>, and kingdoms located in <a href="/wiki/Amu_Darya" title="Amu Darya">Amu Darya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syr_Darya" title="Syr Darya">Syr Darya</a> valley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitfield200447_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitfield200447-120">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000116–118_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000116–118-121">[119]</a></sup> Turkic nomads addressed the Emperor of Tang China as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tian_Kehan" title="Tian Kehan">Tian Kehan</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000124_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000124-32">[30]</a></sup> After the widespread Göktürk revolt of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shabol%C3%BCe_Khan" title="Shabolüe Khan">Shabolüe Khan</a> (d. 658) was put down at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Issyk_Kul" title="Issyk Kul">Issyk Kul</a> in 657 by <a href="/wiki/Su_Dingfang" title="Su Dingfang">Su Dingfang</a> (591–667), Emperor Gaozong established several protectorates governed by a Protectorate General or Grand Protectorate General, which extended the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence" title="Sphere of influence">sphere of influence</a> as far as <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a> in Western Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000118,_122_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000118,_122-122">[120]</a></sup> Protectorate Generals were given a great deal of autonomy to handle local crises without waiting for central admission. After Xuanzong's reign, military governors (jiedushi) were given enormous power, including the ability to maintain their own armies, collect taxes, and pass their titles on hereditarily. This is commonly recognized as the beginning of the fall of Tang's central government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100_54-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006100-54">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang200391_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang200391-63">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E5%94%90%E6%9C%9D%E7%96%86%E5%9F%9F%EF%BC%88%E7%B9%81%EF%BC%89.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1245" data-file-width="1752" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/%E5%94%90%E6%9C%9D%E7%96%86%E5%9F%9F%EF%BC%88%E7%B9%81%EF%BC%89.png/220px-%E5%94%90%E6%9C%9D%E7%96%86%E5%9F%9F%EF%BC%88%E7%B9%81%EF%BC%89.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/%E5%94%90%E6%9C%9D%E7%96%86%E5%9F%9F%EF%BC%88%E7%B9%81%EF%BC%89.png/330px-%E5%94%90%E6%9C%9D%E7%96%86%E5%9F%9F%EF%BC%88%E7%B9%81%EF%BC%89.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/%E5%94%90%E6%9C%9D%E7%96%86%E5%9F%9F%EF%BC%88%E7%B9%81%EF%BC%89.png/440px-%E5%94%90%E6%9C%9D%E7%96%86%E5%9F%9F%EF%BC%88%E7%B9%81%EF%BC%89.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E5%94%90%E6%9C%9D%E7%96%86%E5%9F%9F%EF%BC%88%E7%B9%81%EF%BC%89.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Chinese Tang dynasty during its greatest extension, controlling large parts of Central Asia.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" Tang Zhenguan year 17, i.e. 644 CE</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soldiers_and_conscription">Soldiers and conscription</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Soldiers and conscription">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the year 737, Emperor Xuanzong discarded the policy of conscripting soldiers that were replaced every three years, replacing them with long-service soldiers who were more battle-hardened and efficient. It was more economically feasible as well, since training new recruits and sending them out to the frontier every three years drained the treasury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn20029_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn20029-123">[121]</a></sup> By the late 7th century, the <i><a href="/wiki/Fubing_system" title="Fubing system">fubing</a></i> troops began abandoning military service and the homes provided to them in the equal-field system. The supposed standard of 100 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mu_(unit_of_area)" title="Mu (unit of area)">mu</a></i> of land allotted to each family was in fact decreasing in size in places where population expanded and the wealthy bought up most of the land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraff2002208_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraff2002208-124">[122]</a></sup> Hard-pressed peasants and vagrants were then induced into military service with benefits of exemption from both taxation and corvée labor service, as well as provisions for farmland and dwellings for dependents who accompanied soldiers on the frontier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraff2002209_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraff2002209-125">[123]</a></sup> By the year 742 the total number of enlisted troops in the Tang armies had risen to about 500,000 men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn20029_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn20029-123">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_regions">Eastern regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Eastern regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_General_to_Pacify_the_East" title="Protectorate General to Pacify the East">Protectorate General to Pacify the East</a></div>
<p>In East Asia, Tang Chinese military campaigns were less successful elsewhere than in previous imperial Chinese dynasties. <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo%E2%80%93Sui_War" title="Goguryeo–Sui War">Like the emperors of the Sui dynasty before him</a>, Taizong established a military campaign in 644 against the Korean kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo%E2%80%93Tang_War" title="Goguryeo–Tang War">Goguryeo–Tang War</a>; however, this led to its withdrawal in the <a href="/wiki/First_conflict_of_the_Goguryeo%E2%80%93Tang_War" title="First conflict of the Goguryeo–Tang War">first campaign</a> because they failed to overcome the successful defense led by General <a href="/wiki/Yeon_Gaesomun" title="Yeon Gaesomun">Yeon Gaesomun</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silla%E2%80%93Tang_alliance" title="Silla–Tang alliance">Allying with the Korean Silla Kingdom</a>, the Chinese fought against <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Kofun_period" title="Kofun period">Yamato Japanese</a> allies in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Baekgang" title="Battle of Baekgang">Battle of Baekgang</a> in August 663, a decisive Tang–Silla victory. The Tang dynasty navy had <a href="/wiki/Naval_history_of_China#Tang_era" title="Naval history of China">several different ship types</a> at its disposal to engage in <a href="/wiki/Naval_warfare" title="Naval warfare">naval warfare</a>, these ships described by Li Quan in his <i>Taipai Yinjing</i> (Canon of the White and Gloomy Planet of War) of 759.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c685–687_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c685–687-126">[124]</a></sup> The Battle of Baekgang was actually a restoration movement by remnant forces of Baekje, since their kingdom was toppled in 660 by a joint Tang–Silla invasion, led by Chinese general Su Dingfang and Korean general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kim_Yushin" title="Kim Yushin">Kim Yushin</a> (595–673). In another joint invasion with Silla, the Tang army severely weakened the Goguryeo Kingdom in the north by taking out its outer forts in the year 645. With joint attacks by Silla and Tang armies under commander <a href="/wiki/Li_Shiji" title="Li Shiji">Li Shiji</a> (594–669), the Kingdom of Goguryeo was destroyed by 668.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn20024_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn20024-127">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mogao_Cave_61,_painting_of_Mount_Wutai_monasteries.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1579" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Mogao_Cave_61%2C_painting_of_Mount_Wutai_monasteries.jpg/220px-Mogao_Cave_61%2C_painting_of_Mount_Wutai_monasteries.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Mogao_Cave_61%2C_painting_of_Mount_Wutai_monasteries.jpg/330px-Mogao_Cave_61%2C_painting_of_Mount_Wutai_monasteries.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Mogao_Cave_61%2C_painting_of_Mount_Wutai_monasteries.jpg/440px-Mogao_Cave_61%2C_painting_of_Mount_Wutai_monasteries.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mogao_Cave_61,_painting_of_Mount_Wutai_monasteries.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 10th-century mural painting in the <a href="/wiki/Mogao_Caves" title="Mogao Caves">Mogao Caves</a> at <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang" title="Dunhuang">Dunhuang</a> showing monastic architecture from <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wutai" title="Mount Wutai">Mount Wutai</a>, Tang dynasty; <a href="/wiki/Japanese_architecture" title="Japanese architecture">Japanese architecture</a> of this period was influenced by Tang Chinese architecture</div></div></div>
<p>Although they were formerly enemies, the Tang accepted officials and generals of Goguryeo into their administration and military, such as the brothers <a href="/wiki/Yeon_Namsaeng" title="Yeon Namsaeng">Yeon Namsaeng</a> (634–679) and <a href="/wiki/Yeon_Namsan" title="Yeon Namsan">Yeon Namsan</a> (639–701). From 668 to 676, the Tang Empire would control northern Korea. However, in 671 Silla broke the alliance and began the <a href="/wiki/Silla%E2%80%93Tang_War" title="Silla–Tang War">Silla–Tang War</a> to expel the Tang forces. At the same time the Tang faced threats on its western border when a large Chinese army was defeated by the Tibetans on the Dafei River in 670.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraff2002201_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraff2002201-128">[126]</a></sup> By 676, the Tang army was expelled out of Korea by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Later_Silla" title="Later Silla">Unified Silla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKang200654_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKang200654-129">[127]</a></sup> Following a revolt of the Eastern Turks in 679, the Tang abandoned its Korean campaigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraff2002201_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraff2002201-128">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the Tang had fought the Japanese, they still held cordial relations with Japan. There were numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_embassies_to_China" title="Imperial embassies to China">Imperial embassies to China</a> from Japan, diplomatic missions that were not halted until 894 by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Uda" title="Emperor Uda">Emperor Uda</a> (r. 887–897), upon persuasion by <a href="/wiki/Sugawara_no_Michizane" title="Sugawara no Michizane">Sugawara no Michizane</a> (845–903).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawaTsuchida1975222_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawaTsuchida1975222-130">[128]</a></sup> The Japanese <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Tenmu" title="Emperor Tenmu">Emperor Tenmu</a> (r. 672–686) even established his conscripted army on that of the Chinese model, based his state ceremonies on the Chinese model, and constructed his palace at <a href="/wiki/Fujiwara-ky%C5%8D" title="Fujiwara-kyō">Fujiwara</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_architecture" title="Chinese architecture">Chinese model of architecture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006144_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006144-131">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many Chinese Buddhist monks came to Japan to help further the spread of Buddhism as well. Two 7th-century monks in particular, Zhi Yu and Zhi You, visited the court of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Tenji" title="Emperor Tenji">Emperor Tenji</a> (r. 661–672), whereupon they presented a gift of a <a href="/wiki/South-pointing_chariot" title="South-pointing chariot">south-pointing chariot</a> that they had crafted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b289_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b289-132">[130]</a></sup> This 3rd century mechanically driven directional-<a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compass</a> vehicle (employing a <a href="/wiki/Differential_(mechanical_device)" title="Differential (mechanical device)">differential gear</a>) was again reproduced in several models for Tenji in 666, as recorded in the <i><a href="/wiki/Nihon_Shoki" title="Nihon Shoki">Nihon Shoki</a></i> of 720.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b289_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b289-132">[130]</a></sup> Japanese monks also visited China; such was the case with <a href="/wiki/Ennin" title="Ennin">Ennin</a> (794–864), who wrote of his travel experiences including travels along <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_(China)" title="Grand Canal (China)">China's Grand Canal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c308_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c308-133">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReischauer1940152_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReischauer1940152-134">[132]</a></sup> The Japanese monk <a href="/wiki/Enchin" title="Enchin">Enchin</a> (814–891) stayed in China from 839 to 847 and again from 853 to 858, landing near <a href="/wiki/Fuzhou" title="Fuzhou">Fuzhou, Fujian</a> and setting sail for Japan from <a href="/wiki/Taizhou,_Zhejiang" title="Taizhou, Zhejiang">Taizhou, Zhejiang</a> during his second trip to China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReischauer1940155_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReischauer1940155-135">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200451_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200451-74">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_and_Northern_regions">Western and Northern regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Western and Northern regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_General_to_Pacify_the_West" title="Protectorate General to Pacify the West">Protectorate General to Pacify the West</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_General_to_Pacify_the_North" title="Protectorate General to Pacify the North">Protectorate General to Pacify the North</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_Asia_during_the_Tang_dynasty" title="Inner Asia during the Tang dynasty">Inner Asia during the Tang dynasty</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tibet_and_the_Tang_and_Song_dynasties" title="Tibet and the Tang and Song dynasties">Tibet and the Tang and Song dynasties</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tang_Pottery_Horse_%26_Rider2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3168" data-file-width="4752" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Tang_Pottery_Horse_%26_Rider2.jpg/220px-Tang_Pottery_Horse_%26_Rider2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Tang_Pottery_Horse_%26_Rider2.jpg/330px-Tang_Pottery_Horse_%26_Rider2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Tang_Pottery_Horse_%26_Rider2.jpg/440px-Tang_Pottery_Horse_%26_Rider2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tang_Pottery_Horse_%26_Rider2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb figure of mounted warrior similar to the one unearthed from the tomb of Crown Prince <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li_Chongrun" title="Li Chongrun">Li Chongrun</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Sui and Tang carried out successful military campaigns against the steppe nomads. Chinese foreign policy to the north and west now had to deal with <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> nomads, who were becoming the most dominant ethnic group in Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006113_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006113-136">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEXue1992149–152,_257–264_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEXue1992149–152,_257–264-137">[135]</a></sup> To handle and avoid any threats posed by the Turks, the Sui government repaired fortifications and received their trade and tribute missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200692_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200692-102">[100]</a></sup> They sent four royal princesses to form <a href="/wiki/Heqin" title="Heqin">marriage alliances</a> with Turkic clan leaders, in 597, 599, 614, and 617. The Sui stirred trouble and conflict amongst ethnic groups against the Turks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn20022–3_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn20022–3-138">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECui2005655–659_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECui2005655–659-139">[137]</a></sup> As early as the Sui dynasty, the Turks had become <a href="/wiki/Turks_in_the_Tang_military" title="Turks in the Tang military">a major militarized force employed by the Chinese</a>. When the <a href="/wiki/Khitan_people" title="Khitan people">Khitans</a> began raiding northeast China in 605, a Chinese general led 20,000 Turks against them, distributing Khitan livestock and women to the Turks as a reward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999111_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999111-140">[138]</a></sup> On two occasions between 635 and 636, Tang royal princesses were married to Turk mercenaries or generals in Chinese service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECui2005655–659_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECui2005655–659-139">[137]</a></sup> Throughout the Tang dynasty until the end of 755, there were approximately ten Turkic generals serving under the Tang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEXue1992788_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEXue1992788-141">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000125_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000125-142">[140]</a></sup> While most of the Tang army was made of <i>fubing</i> Chinese conscripts, the majority of the troops led by Turkic generals were of non-Chinese origin, campaigning largely in the western frontier where the presence of <i>fubing</i> troops was low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiu200085–95_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiu200085–95-143">[141]</a></sup> Some "Turkic" troops were tribalized Han Chinese, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desinicization" title="Desinicization">desinicized</a> people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1996248_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1996248-144">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>Civil war in China was almost totally diminished by 626, along with the defeat in 628 of the <a href="/wiki/Ordos_Desert" title="Ordos Desert">Ordos</a> Chinese warlord <a href="/wiki/Liang_Shidu" title="Liang Shidu">Liang Shidu</a>; after these internal conflicts, the Tang began an offensive against the Turks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEXue1992226–227_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEXue1992226–227-145">[143]</a></sup> In the year 630, Tang armies captured areas of the Ordos Desert, modern-day <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a> province, and southern <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> from the Turks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999111_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999111-140">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEXue1992380–386_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEXue1992380–386-146">[144]</a></sup> After this military victory, On June 11, 631, Emperor Taizong also sent envoys to the <a href="/wiki/Xueyantuo" title="Xueyantuo">Xueyantuo</a> bearing gold and silk in order to persuade the release of enslaved Chinese prisoners who were captured during the <a href="/wiki/Transition_from_Sui_to_Tang" title="Transition from Sui to Tang">transition from Sui to Tang</a> from the northern frontier; this embassy succeeded in freeing 80,000 Chinese men and women who were then returned to China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn20022_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn20022-147">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEXue1992222–225_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEXue1992222–225-148">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_162.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3368" data-file-width="1556" decoding="async" height="368" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_162.jpg/170px-Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_162.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_162.jpg/255px-Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_162.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_162.jpg/340px-Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_162.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_162.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty_tomb_figures" title="Tang dynasty tomb figures">Tomb guardian</a> (<i>wushi yong</i>), early 8th century</div></div></div>
<p>While the Turks were settled in the Ordos region (former territory of the <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a>), the Tang government took on the military policy of dominating the central <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a>. Like the earlier Han dynasty, the Tang dynasty (along with Turkic allies) conquered and subdued Central Asia during the 640s and 650s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200692_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200692-102">[100]</a></sup> During Emperor Taizong's reign alone, large campaigns were launched against not only the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%B6kt%C3%BCrk" title="Göktürk">Göktürks</a>, but also separate campaigns against the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong%27s_campaign_against_Tuyuhun" title="Emperor Taizong's campaign against Tuyuhun">Tuyuhun</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_campaign_against_the_oasis_states" title="Tang campaign against the oasis states">oasis city-states</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong%27s_campaign_against_Xueyantuo" title="Emperor Taizong's campaign against Xueyantuo">Xueyantuo</a>. Under Emperor Gaozong, a campaign led by the general <a href="/wiki/Su_Dingfang" title="Su Dingfang">Su Dingfang</a> was <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_the_Western_Turks" title="Conquest of the Western Turks">launched against the Western Turks</a> ruled by Ashina Helu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkaff2009183_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkaff2009183-149">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Tang Empire competed with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibetan_Kingdom" title="Tibetan Kingdom">Tibetan Empire</a> for control of areas in Inner and Central Asia, which was at times settled with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marriage_alliances" title="Marriage alliances">marriage alliances</a> such as the marrying of <a href="/wiki/Princess_Wencheng" title="Princess Wencheng">Princess Wencheng</a> (d. 680) to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Songts%C3%A4n_Gampo" title="Songtsän Gampo">Songtsän Gampo</a> (d. 649).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitfield2004193_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitfield2004193-150">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESen200324,_30–31_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESen200324,_30–31-151">[149]</a></sup> A Tibetan tradition mentions that Chinese troops captured Lhasa after Songtsän Gampo's death,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup> but no such invasion is mentioned in either Chinese annals or the Tibetan manuscripts of <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang" title="Dunhuang">Dunhuang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>
There was a long string of conflicts with Tibet over territories in the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a> between 670 and 692, and in 763 the Tibetans even captured the capital of China, <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a>, for fifteen days during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/An_Shi_Rebellion" title="An Shi Rebellion">An Shi Rebellion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckwith1987146_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckwith1987146-154">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStein197265_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStein197265-155">[153]</a></sup> In fact, it was during this rebellion that the Tang withdrew its western garrisons stationed in what is now <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qinghai" title="Qinghai">Qinghai</a>, which the Tibetans then occupied along with the territory of what is now <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000109_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000109-156">[154]</a></sup> Hostilities between the Tang and Tibet continued until they signed a formal peace treaty in 821.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200211_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200211-157">[155]</a></sup> The terms of this treaty, including the fixed borders between the two countries, are recorded in a bilingual inscription on <a href="/wiki/Tang%E2%80%93Tibet_Treaty_Inscription" title="Tang–Tibet Treaty Inscription">a stone pillar</a> outside the <a href="/wiki/Jokhang" title="Jokhang">Jokhang</a> temple in Lhasa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichardson1985106–143_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichardson1985106–143-158">[156]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Taizongs_horses_by_Yan_Liben.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="623" data-file-width="709" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Emperor_Taizongs_horses_by_Yan_Liben.jpg/220px-Emperor_Taizongs_horses_by_Yan_Liben.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Emperor_Taizongs_horses_by_Yan_Liben.jpg/330px-Emperor_Taizongs_horses_by_Yan_Liben.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Emperor_Taizongs_horses_by_Yan_Liben.jpg/440px-Emperor_Taizongs_horses_by_Yan_Liben.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Taizongs_horses_by_Yan_Liben.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bas relief of a soldier and the emperor's horse, <a href="/wiki/Autumn_Dew" title="Autumn Dew">Autumn Dew</a>, with elaborate <a href="/wiki/Saddle" title="Saddle">saddle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stirrup" title="Stirrup">stirrups</a>, designed by <a href="/wiki/Yan_Liben" title="Yan Liben">Yan Liben</a>, from the tomb of Emperor Taizong c. 650</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Persia" title="Islamic conquest of Persia">Islamic conquest of Persia</a> (633–656), the son of the last ruler of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peroz_III" title="Peroz III">Prince Peroz</a> and his court moved to Tang China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitfield200447_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitfield200447-120">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198510,_25–26_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198510,_25–26-159">[157]</a></sup> According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Book_of_Tang" title="Old Book of Tang">Old Book of Tang</a></i>, Peroz was made the head of a Governorate of Persia in what is now <a href="/wiki/Zaranj" title="Zaranj">Zaranj</a>, Afghanistan. During this conquest of Persia, the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">Rashidun</a> Caliph <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uthman_Ibn_Affan" title="Uthman Ibn Affan">Uthman Ibn Affan</a> (r. 644–656) sent an embassy to the Tang court at Chang'an.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000125_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000125-142">[140]</a></sup> Arab sources claim Umayyad commander <a href="/wiki/Qutayba_ibn_Muslim" title="Qutayba ibn Muslim">Qutayba ibn Muslim</a> briefly took <a href="/wiki/Shule_Kingdom" title="Shule Kingdom">Kashgar</a> from China and withdrew after an agreement,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olimat2015_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olimat2015-160">[158]</a></sup> but modern historians entirely dismiss this claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup> The Arab <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a> in 715 deposed <a href="/wiki/Ikhshid" title="Ikhshid">Ikhshid</a>, the king the <a href="/wiki/Fergana_Valley" title="Fergana Valley">Fergana Valley</a>, and installed a new king Alutar on the throne. The deposed king fled to <a href="/wiki/Kucha" title="Kucha">Kucha</a> (seat of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anxi_Protectorate" title="Anxi Protectorate">Anxi Protectorate</a>), and sought Chinese intervention. The Chinese sent 10,000 troops under <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Zhang_Xiaosong&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zhang Xiaosong (page does not exist)">Zhang Xiaosong</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferghana" title="Ferghana">Ferghana</a>. He defeated Alutar and the Arab occupation force at <a href="/wiki/Namangan" title="Namangan">Namangan</a> and reinstalled Ikhshid on the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bai_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-bai-164">[162]</a></sup> The Tang dynasty Chinese defeated the Arab <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad</a> invaders at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aksu_(717)" title="Battle of Aksu (717)">Battle of Aksu (717)</a>. The Arab Umayyad commander Al-Yashkuri and his army fled to Tashkent after they were defeated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith1993_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith1993-165">[163]</a></sup> The Turgesh then <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Transoxiana#Umayyad–Turgesh_Wars" title="Muslim conquest of Transoxiana">crushed the Arab Umayyads</a> and drove them out. By the 740s, the <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a> had reestablished a presence in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferghana" title="Ferghana">Ferghana</a> basin and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a>. At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Talas" title="Battle of Talas">Battle of Talas</a> in 751, <a href="/wiki/Karluks" title="Karluks">Karluk</a> mercenaries under the Chinese defected, helping the Arab armies of the Caliphate to defeat the Tang force under commander <a href="/wiki/Gao_Xianzhi" title="Gao Xianzhi">Gao Xianzhi</a>. Although the battle itself was not of the greatest significance militarily, this was a pivotal moment in history, as it marks the spread of Chinese <a href="/wiki/Papermaking" title="Papermaking">papermaking</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBai2003242–243_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBai2003242–243-166">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005183_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005183-167">[165]</a></sup> into regions west of China as captured Chinese soldiers shared the technique of papermaking to the Arabs. These techniques ultimately reached Europe by the 12th century through <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Arab-controlled Spain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuller_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-fuller-168">[166]</a></sup> Although they had fought at Talas, on June 11, 758, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasid" title="Abbasid">Abbasid</a> embassy arrived at Chang'an simultaneously with the Uighur Turks bearing gifts for the Tang Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198526_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198526-169">[167]</a></sup> In 788–789 the Chinese concluded a military alliance with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uyghur_people" title="Uyghur people">Uighur</a> Turks who twice defeated the Tibetans, in 789 near the town of <a href="/wiki/Gaochang" title="Gaochang">Gaochang</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dzungaria" title="Dzungaria">Dzungaria</a>, and in 791 near <a href="/wiki/Ningxia" title="Ningxia">Ningxia</a> on the Yellow River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="718" data-file-width="898" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg/220px-Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg/330px-Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg/440px-Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_of_Byzantine_embassy_to_Tang_Taizong_643_CE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of Byzantine embassy to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Taizong" title="Tang Taizong">Tang Taizong</a> 643 CE</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Needham" title="Joseph Needham">Joseph Needham</a> writes that a <a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/s:%E6%96%B0%E5%94%90%E6%9B%B8/%E5%8D%B7221%E4%B8%8B" title="zh:s:新唐書/卷221下">tributary embassy came to the court of Emperor Taizong in 643</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Patriarchs_of_Antioch" title="List of Patriarchs of Antioch">Patriarch of Antioch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b476_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b476-171">[169]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hirth" title="Friedrich Hirth">Friedrich Hirth</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Sinology" title="Sinology">sinologists</a> such as S.A.M. Adshead have identified <i>Fu lin</i> (拂菻) in the <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Book_of_Tang" title="Old Book of Tang">Old</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/New_Book_of_Tang" title="New Book of Tang">New Book of Tang</a></i> as the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, which those histories directly associated with <i><a href="/wiki/Daqin" title="Daqin">Daqin</a></i> (i.e. the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead1995104–106_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead1995104–106-172">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-173">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYule191554–55_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYule191554–55-174">[172]</a></sup> The embassy sent in 643 by <i>Boduoli</i> (波多力) was identified as Byzantine ruler <a href="/wiki/Constans_II" title="Constans II">Constans II Pogonatos</a> (<i>Kōnstantinos Pogonatos</i>, or "Constantine the Bearded") and <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">further embassies</a> were recorded as being sent into the 8th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-173">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYule191554–55_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYule191554–55-174">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead1995104–106_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead1995104–106-172">[170]</a></sup> S.A.M. Adshead offers a different transliteration stemming from "<a href="/wiki/Patriarch" title="Patriarch">patriarch</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Patrician_(ancient_Rome)" title="Patrician (ancient Rome)">patrician</a>", possibly a reference to one of the acting <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regents</a> for the young Byzantine monarch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead1995105_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead1995105-175">[173]</a></sup> The <i>Old</i> and <i>New Book of Tang</i> also provide a description of the Byzantine capital <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall2016152–153,_see_endnote_114_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall2016152–153,_see_endnote_114-176">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYule191546–48_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYule191546–48-177">[175]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(674%E2%80%93678)" title="Siege of Constantinople (674–678)">how it was besieged</a> by the <i>Da shi</i> (大食, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a>) forces of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">Muawiyah I</a>, who forced them to pay tribute to the Arabs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_173-2"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-173">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYule191548–49_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYule191548–49-178">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[c]</a></sup> The 7th-century Byzantine historian <a href="/wiki/Theophylact_Simocatta" title="Theophylact Simocatta">Theophylact Simocatta</a> wrote about the reunification of <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">northern and southern China</a> by the Sui dynasty (dating this to the time of <a href="/wiki/Maurice_(emperor)" title="Maurice (emperor)">Emperor Maurice</a>); the capital city <i>Khubdan</i> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Turkic_language" title="Old Turkic language">Old Turkic</a> <i>Khumdan</i>, i.e. Chang'an); the basic <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_China" title="Geography of China">geography of China</a> including its previous political division around the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a>; the name of China's ruler <i>Taisson</i> meaning "<a href="/wiki/Son_of_Heaven" title="Son of Heaven">Son of God</a>", but possibly derived from the name of the contemporaneous ruler Emperor Taizong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYule191529–31_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYule191529–31-180">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:150px;max-width:150px"><div class="thumbimage" style="border:none;;height:197px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gilt_silver_jar_with_pattern_of_dancing_horses.jpg"><img alt="A trout" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Gilt_silver_jar_with_pattern_of_dancing_horses.jpg/148px-Gilt_silver_jar_with_pattern_of_dancing_horses.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Gilt_silver_jar_with_pattern_of_dancing_horses.jpg/222px-Gilt_silver_jar_with_pattern_of_dancing_horses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Gilt_silver_jar_with_pattern_of_dancing_horses.jpg/296px-Gilt_silver_jar_with_pattern_of_dancing_horses.jpg 2x" width="148"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A Tang period <a href="/wiki/Gilding" title="Gilding">gilt</a>-silver jar, shaped in the style of northern <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomad</a>'s leather bag<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999127_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999127-181">[178]</a></sup> decorated with a <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_the_horse" title="Domestication of the horse">horse</a> dancing with a cup of wine in its mouth, as the horses of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuanzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuanzong of Tang">Emperor Xuanzong</a> were trained to do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999127_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999127-181">[178]</a></sup></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Through use of the land trade along the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maritime_history" title="Maritime history">maritime trade</a> by sail at sea, the Tang were able to acquire and gain many new technologies, cultural practices, rare luxury, and contemporary items. From Europe, the Middle East, Central and South Asia, the Tang dynasty were able to acquire new ideas in fashion, new types of ceramics, and improved silver-smithing techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999118–119_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999118–119-182">[179]</a></sup> The Tang Chinese also gradually adopted the foreign concept of stools and chairs as seating, whereas the Chinese beforehand always sat on mats placed on the floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999119_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999119-183">[180]</a></sup> People of the Middle East coveted and purchased in bulk Chinese goods such as silks, <a href="/wiki/Lacquerware" title="Lacquerware">lacquerwares</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a> wares.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006112_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006112-184">[181]</a></sup> Songs, dances, and musical instruments from foreign regions became popular in China during the Tang dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006114_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006114-185">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitfield2004255_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitfield2004255-186">[183]</a></sup> These musical instruments included <a href="/wiki/Oboe" title="Oboe">oboes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flutes</a>, and small lacquered drums from <a href="/wiki/Kucha" title="Kucha">Kucha</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a>, and percussion instruments from India such as <a href="/wiki/Cymbal" title="Cymbal">cymbals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006114_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006114-185">[182]</a></sup> At the court there were nine musical ensembles (expanded from seven in the Sui dynasty) that played ecletic Asian music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002134_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002134-187">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qianfengquanbao_and_Qianyuanzhongbao.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1820" data-file-width="2736" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Qianfengquanbao_and_Qianyuanzhongbao.jpg/220px-Qianfengquanbao_and_Qianyuanzhongbao.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Qianfengquanbao_and_Qianyuanzhongbao.jpg/330px-Qianfengquanbao_and_Qianyuanzhongbao.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Qianfengquanbao_and_Qianyuanzhongbao.jpg/440px-Qianfengquanbao_and_Qianyuanzhongbao.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qianfengquanbao_and_Qianyuanzhongbao.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tang dynasty <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kai_Yuan_Tong_Bao" title="Kai Yuan Tong Bao">Kai Yuan Tong Bao</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-TW">開元通寳</span></span>) <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_coinage" title="Ancient Chinese coinage">coin</a>, first minted in 621 in <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a>, a model for the Japanese 8th-century <i><a href="/wiki/Wad%C5%8Dkaichin" title="Wadōkaichin">Wadōkaichin</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>There was great interaction with India, a hub for Buddhist knowledge, with famous travelers such as <a href="/wiki/Xuanzang" title="Xuanzang">Xuanzang</a> (d. 664) visiting the South Asian state. After a 17-year-long trip, Xuanzang managed to bring back valuable <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> texts to be translated into Chinese. There was also a <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a>–Chinese dictionary available for serious scholars and students, while Turkic folk songs gave inspiration to some Chinese poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198528_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198528-188">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005182_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005182-189">[186]</a></sup> In the interior of China, trade was facilitated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_of_China" title="Grand Canal of China">Grand Canal</a> and the Tang government's rationalization of the greater canal system that reduced costs of transporting grain and other commodities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn20027_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn20027-50">[48]</a></sup> The state also managed roughly 32,100 km (19,900 mi) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postal_administration" title="Postal administration">postal service</a> routes by horse or boat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200490_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200490-190">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silk_Road">Silk Road</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Silk Road">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> from China to <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Western_civilization" title="Western civilization">Europe and the Western World</a> was initially formulated during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu</a> (141–87 BC) during the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a>, it was reopened by the Tang in 639 when <a href="/wiki/Hou_Junji" title="Hou Junji">Hou Junji</a> (<abbr title="Died">d.</abbr> 643) conquered the West, and remained open for almost four decades. It was closed after the Tibetans captured it in 678, but in 699, during Empress Wu's period, the Silk Road reopened when the Tang reconquered the <a href="/wiki/Four_Garrisons_of_Anxi" title="Four Garrisons of Anxi">Four Garrisons of Anxi</a> originally installed in 640,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000118_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwitchett2000118-191">[188]</a></sup> once again connecting China directly to the West for land-based trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005179_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005179-192">[189]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tang_Sancai_Porcelain_with_Musicians_on_a_Camel_(no_background).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4676" data-file-width="2982" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Tang_Sancai_Porcelain_with_Musicians_on_a_Camel_%28no_background%29.jpg/170px-Tang_Sancai_Porcelain_with_Musicians_on_a_Camel_%28no_background%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Tang_Sancai_Porcelain_with_Musicians_on_a_Camel_%28no_background%29.jpg/255px-Tang_Sancai_Porcelain_with_Musicians_on_a_Camel_%28no_background%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Tang_Sancai_Porcelain_with_Musicians_on_a_Camel_%28no_background%29.jpg/340px-Tang_Sancai_Porcelain_with_Musicians_on_a_Camel_%28no_background%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tang_Sancai_Porcelain_with_Musicians_on_a_Camel_(no_background).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Tang Dynasty <i><a href="/wiki/Sancai" title="Sancai">sancai</a></i> statuette of <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdian</a> musicians riding on a <a href="/wiki/Bactrian_camel" title="Bactrian camel">Bactrian camel</a>, 723 AD, <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>.</div></div></div>
