<p>The Tang captured the vital route through the <a href="/wiki/Gilgit" title="Gilgit">Gilgit Valley</a> from Tibet in 722, lost it to the Tibetans in 737, and regained it under the command of the Goguryeo-Korean General Gao Xianzhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESen200330–32_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESen200330–32-193">[190]</a></sup> When the An Lushan Rebellion ended in 763, the Tang Empire withdrew its troops from its western lands, allowing the Tibetan Empire to largely cut off China's direct access to the Silk Road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200211_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200211-157">[155]</a></sup> An internal rebellion in 848 ousted the Tibetan rulers, and Tang China regained its northwestern prefectures from Tibet in 851. These lands contained crucial grazing areas and pastures for raising horses that the Tang dynasty desperately needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200211_157-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200211-157">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitfield200457,_228_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitfield200457,_228-194">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the many expatriate European travelers coming into China to live and trade, many travelers, mainly religious monks and missionaries, recorded China's stringent immigrant laws. As the monk Xuanzang and many other monk travelers attested to, there were many Chinese government <a href="/wiki/Border_checkpoint" title="Border checkpoint">checkpoints</a> along the Silk Road that examined travel permits into the Tang Empire. Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Outlaw" title="Outlaw">banditry</a> was a problem along the checkpoints and <a href="/wiki/Oasis" title="Oasis">oasis</a> towns, as Xuanzang also recorded that his group of travelers were assaulted by bandits on multiple occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006112_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006112-184">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> also affected <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty_paintings&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tang dynasty paintings (page does not exist)">Tang dynasty art</a>. Horses became a significant symbol of prosperity and power as well as an instrument of military and diplomatic policy. Horses were also revered as a relative of the dragon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seaports_and_maritime_trade">Seaports and maritime trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Seaports and maritime trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="903" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg/170px-Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg/255px-Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg/340px-Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A contract from the Tang dynasty that records the purchase of a 15-year-old slave for six bolts of plain <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> and five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_coins" title="Chinese coins">Chinese coins</a>. Found in the <a href="/wiki/Astana_Cemetery" title="Astana Cemetery">Astana Cemetery</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turfan" title="Turfan">Turfan</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Chinese envoys had been sailing through the Indian Ocean to states of <a href="/wiki/Kanchipuram" title="Kanchipuram">India</a> since perhaps the 2nd century BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESun1989161–167_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESun1989161–167-196">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen200267–71_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen200267–71-197">[194]</a></sup> yet it was during the Tang dynasty that a strong Chinese maritime presence could be found in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>, into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (sailing up the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates River</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, Egypt in the Middle East and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aksum" title="Aksum">Aksum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowman2000104–105_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowman2000104–105-198">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Tang dynasty, thousands of foreign expatriate merchants came and lived in numerous Chinese cities to do business with China, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> Indians, <a href="/wiki/Malays_(ethnic_group)" title="Malays (ethnic group)">Malays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bengalis" title="Bengalis">Bengalis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinhalese_people" title="Sinhalese people">Sinhalese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khmer_people" title="Khmer people">Khmers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cham_(Asia)" title="Cham (Asia)">Chams</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_China" title="History of the Jews in China">Jews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorian Christians</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, among many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200246_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200246-199">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198520_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198520-200">[197]</a></sup> In 748, the Buddhist monk Jian Zhen described <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> as a bustling mercantile business center where many large and impressive foreign ships came to dock. He wrote that "many large ships came from <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>, Persia, Qunglun (<a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>/<a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>)<span class="nowrap"> </span>... with<span class="nowrap"> </span>... spices, pearls, and jade piled up mountain high",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETang199161_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETang199161-201">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198515_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198515-202">[199]</a></sup> as written in the <i>Yue Jue Shu</i> (Lost Records of the State of Yue). Relations with the Arabs were often strained: When the imperial government was attempting to quell the <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan_Rebellion" title="An Lushan Rebellion">An Lushan Rebellion</a>, Arab and Persian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pirate" title="Pirate">pirates</a> burned and looted Canton on October 30, 758. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200211_157-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200211-157">[155]</a></sup> The Tang government reacted by shutting the port of Canton down for roughly five decades; thus, foreign vessels docked at <a href="/wiki/Hanoi" title="Hanoi">Hanoi</a> instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198516_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198516-203">[200]</a></sup> However, when the port reopened, it continued to thrive. In 851 the Arab merchant <a href="/wiki/Sulaiman_al-Tajir" title="Sulaiman al-Tajir">Sulaiman al-Tajir</a> observed the manufacturing of Chinese <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a> in Guangzhou and admired its transparent quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShen1996163_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShen1996163-204">[201]</a></sup> He also provided a description of Guangzhou's landmarks, granaries, local government administration, some of its written records, treatment of travelers, along with the use of <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramics</a>, rice, wine, and tea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoods1996143_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoods1996143-205">[202]</a></sup> Their presence <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou_massacre" title="Guangzhou massacre">came to an end</a> under the revenge of Chinese rebel Huang Chao in 878, who purportedly slaughtered thousands regardless of ethnicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006108_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006108-82">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198510,_16_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198510,_16-206">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005190_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005190-207">[204]</a></sup> Huang's rebellion was eventually suppressed in 884.
</p><p>Vessels from neighboring East Asian states such as Silla and <a href="/wiki/Balhae" title="Balhae">Balhae</a> of Korea and the <a href="/wiki/Hizen_Province" title="Hizen Province">Hizen Province</a> of Japan were all involved in the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Sea" title="Yellow Sea">Yellow Sea</a> trade, which Silla dominated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198511_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198511-208">[205]</a></sup> After Silla and Japan reopened renewed hostilities in the late 7th century, most Japanese maritime merchants chose to set sail from <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki_Prefecture" title="Nagasaki Prefecture">Nagasaki</a> towards the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Huai_River" title="Huai River">Huai River</a>, the Yangtze River, and even as far south as the <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou_Bay" title="Hangzhou Bay">Hangzhou Bay</a> in order to avoid Korean ships in the Yellow Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198511_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198511-208">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReischauer1940157_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReischauer1940157-209">[206]</a></sup> In order to sail back to Japan in 838, the Japanese embassy to China procured nine ships and sixty Korean sailors from the Korean wards of Chuzhou and Lianshui cities along the Huai River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReischauer1940162_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReischauer1940162-210">[207]</a></sup> It is also known that Chinese trade ships traveling to Japan set sail from the various ports along the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReischauer1940155–156_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReischauer1940155–156-211">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Chinese engaged in large-scale production for overseas export by at least the time of the Tang. This was proven by the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Belitung_shipwreck" title="Belitung shipwreck">Belitung shipwreck</a>, a silt-preserved shipwrecked Arabian <a href="/wiki/Dhow" title="Dhow">dhow</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Gaspar_Strait" title="Gaspar Strait">Gaspar Strait</a> near <a href="/wiki/Belitung" title="Belitung">Belitung</a>, which had 63,000 pieces of Tang ceramics, silver, and gold (including a <a href="/wiki/Changsha" title="Changsha">Changsha</a> bowl inscribed with a date: "16th day of the seventh month of the second year of the Baoli reign", or 826, roughly confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">radiocarbon dating</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_anise" title="Star anise">star anise</a> at the wreck).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_news_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_news-212">[209]</a></sup> Beginning in 785, the Chinese began to call regularly at <a href="/wiki/Sufala" title="Sufala">Sufala</a> on the East African coast in order to cut out Arab middlemen,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShen1996155_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShen1996155-213">[210]</a></sup> with various contemporary Chinese sources giving detailed descriptions of trade in Africa. The official and geographer <a href="/wiki/Jia_Dan" title="Jia Dan">Jia Dan</a> (730–805) wrote of two common sea trade routes in his day: one from the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Bohai_Sea" title="Bohai Sea">Bohai Sea</a> towards Korea and another from Guangzhou through <a href="/wiki/Malacca" title="Malacca">Malacca</a> towards the <a href="/wiki/Nicobar_Islands" title="Nicobar Islands">Nicobar Islands</a>, Sri Lanka and India, the eastern and northern shores of the Arabian Sea to the Euphrates River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHsu198896_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHsu198896-214">[211]</a></sup> In 863 the Chinese author <a href="/wiki/Duan_Chengshi" title="Duan Chengshi">Duan Chengshi</a> (d. 863) provided a detailed description of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivory_trade" title="Ivory trade">ivory trade</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ambergris" title="Ambergris">ambergris</a> trade in a country called <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bobali_(Chinese_geography)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bobali (Chinese geography) (page does not exist)">Bobali</a>, which historians suggest was <a href="/wiki/Berbera" title="Berbera">Berbera</a> in Somalia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevathes199438_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevathes199438-215">[212]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Fustat" title="Fustat">Fustat</a> (old <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>), Egypt, the fame of Chinese ceramics there led to an enormous demand for Chinese goods; hence Chinese often traveled there (this continued into later periods such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimid" title="Fatimid">Fatimid</a> Egypt).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShen1996158_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShen1996158-216">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200480_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200480-217">[214]</a></sup> From this time period, the Arab merchant Shulama once wrote of his admiration for Chinese seafaring <a href="/wiki/Junk_(ship)" title="Junk (ship)">junks</a>, but noted that their draft was too deep for them to enter the Euphrates River, which forced them to ferry passengers and cargo in small boats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiu1991178_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiu1991178-218">[215]</a></sup> Shulama also noted that Chinese ships were often very large, with capacities up to 600–700 passengers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHsu198896_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHsu198896-214">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiu1991178_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiu1991178-218">[215]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture_and_society">Culture and society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Culture and society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty_art" title="Tang dynasty art">Tang dynasty art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:1208px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EightySevenCelestials3.jpg" title="Eighty Seven Celestials, draft painting of a fresco by Wu Daozi (c. 685–758)"><img alt="" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="2848" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/EightySevenCelestials3.jpg/1200px-EightySevenCelestials3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/EightySevenCelestials3.jpg/1800px-EightySevenCelestials3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/EightySevenCelestials3.jpg/2400px-EightySevenCelestials3.jpg 2x" width="1200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EightySevenCelestials3.jpg" title="File:EightySevenCelestials3.jpg"> </a></div><i>Eighty Seven Celestials</i>, draft painting of a fresco by <a href="/wiki/Wu_Daozi" title="Wu Daozi">Wu Daozi</a> (c. 685–758)</div></div></div>
<p>Both the Sui and Tang Dynasties had turned away from the more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feudal" title="Feudal">feudal</a> culture of the preceding Northern Dynasties, in favor of staunch civil <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691_7-11"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200691-7">[6]</a></sup> The governmental system was supported by a large class of Confucian intellectuals selected through either civil service examinations or recommendations. In the Tang period, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> were commonly practiced ideologies that played a large role in people's daily lives. The Tang Chinese enjoyed feasting, drinking, holidays, sports, and all sorts of entertainment, while <a href="/wiki/Chinese_literature" title="Chinese literature">Chinese literature</a> blossomed and was more widely accessible with new <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a> methods.
</p>
<h3><span id="Chang.27an.2C_the_Tang_capital"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chang'an,_the_Tang_capital">Chang'an, the Tang capital</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Chang'an, the Tang capital">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prince_Yide%27s_tomb,_towers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2267" data-file-width="1952" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Prince_Yide%27s_tomb%2C_towers.jpg/170px-Prince_Yide%27s_tomb%2C_towers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Prince_Yide%27s_tomb%2C_towers.jpg/255px-Prince_Yide%27s_tomb%2C_towers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Prince_Yide%27s_tomb%2C_towers.jpg/340px-Prince_Yide%27s_tomb%2C_towers.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prince_Yide%27s_tomb,_towers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mural depicting a <a href="/wiki/Chinese_city_wall" title="Chinese city wall">corner tower</a>, most likely one of <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a>, from the tomb of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li_Chongrun" title="Li Chongrun">Prince Yide</a> (d. 701) at the <a href="/wiki/Qianling_Mausoleum" title="Qianling Mausoleum">Qianling Mausoleum</a>, dated 706</div></div></div>
<p>Although Chang'an was the capital of the earlier Han and Jin dynasties, after subsequent destruction in warfare, it was the Sui dynasty model that comprised the Tang era capital. The roughly square dimensions of the city had six miles (10 km) of outer walls running east to west, and more than five miles (8 km) of outer walls running north to south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200693_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200693-30">[28]</a></sup> The royal palace, the Taiji Palace, stood north of the city's central axis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[216]</a></sup> From the large Mingde Gates located mid-center of the main southern wall, a wide city avenue stretched from there all the way north to the central administrative city, behind which was the Chentian Gate of the royal palace, or Imperial City. Intersecting this were fourteen main streets running east to west, while eleven main streets ran north to south. These main intersecting roads formed 108 rectangular wards with walls and four gates each, and each ward filled with multiple <a href="/wiki/City_block" title="City block">city blocks</a>. The city was made famous for this checkerboard pattern of main roads with walled and gated districts, its layout even mentioned in one of Du Fu's poems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006103_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006103-220">[217]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Heian_period" title="Heian period">Heian period</a>, the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heian_ky%C5%8D" title="Heian kyō">Heian kyō</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>) of Japan like many cities was arranged in the checkerboard street grid pattern of the Tang capital and in accordance with traditional geomancy following the model of Chang'an.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200692_102-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200692-102">[100]</a></sup> Of these 108 wards in Chang'an, two of them (each the size of two regular city wards) were designated as government-supervised markets, and other space reserved for temples, gardens, ponds, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200693_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200693-30">[28]</a></sup> Throughout the entire city, there were 111 Buddhist monasteries, 41 Taoist abbeys, 38 family shrines, 2 official temples, 7 churches of foreign religions, 10 city wards with provincial transmission offices, 12 major inns, and 6 graveyards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002xiii_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002xiii-221">[218]</a></sup> Some city wards were literally filled with open public playing fields or the backyards of lavish mansions for playing horse polo and <a href="/wiki/Cuju" title="Cuju">cuju</a> (Chinese soccer).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002xiv,_xv,_xvi,_xvii,_xviii_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002xiv,_xv,_xvi,_xvii,_xviii-222">[219]</a></sup> In 662, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Gaozong of Tang">Emperor Gaozong</a> moved the imperial court to the <a href="/wiki/Daming_Palace" title="Daming Palace">Daming Palace</a>, which became the political center of the empire and served as the royal residence of the Tang emperors for more than 220 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[220]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chang%27an_of_Tang.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="728" data-file-width="983" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Chang%27an_of_Tang.jpg/300px-Chang%27an_of_Tang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Chang%27an_of_Tang.jpg/450px-Chang%27an_of_Tang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Chang%27an_of_Tang.jpg/600px-Chang%27an_of_Tang.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chang%27an_of_Tang.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Chang'an in Tang Dynasty</div></div></div>
<p>The Tang capital was the largest city in the world at its time, the population of the city wards and its suburban countryside reaching two million inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200693_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200693-30">[28]</a></sup> The Tang capital was very cosmopolitan, with ethnicities of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>, Central Asia, Japan, Korea, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, Tibet, India, and many other places living within. Naturally, with this plethora of different ethnicities living in Chang'an, there were also many different practiced religions, such as <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorian Christianity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200679_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200679-224">[221]</a></sup> With the open access to China that the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> to the west facilitated, many foreign settlers were able to move east to China, while the city of Chang'an itself had about 25,000 foreigners living within.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006112_184-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006112-184">[181]</a></sup> Exotic green-eyed, blond-haired <a href="/wiki/Tocharians" title="Tocharians">Tocharian ladies</a> serving wine in <a href="/wiki/Agate" title="Agate">agate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a> cups, singing, and dancing at taverns attracted customers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198521_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198521-225">[222]</a></sup> If a foreigner in China pursued a Chinese woman for marriage, he was required to stay in China and was unable to take his bride back to his homeland, as stated in a law passed in 628 to protect women from temporary marriages with foreign envoys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198525_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198525-226">[223]</a></sup> Several laws enforcing segregation of foreigners from Chinese were passed during the Tang dynasty. In 779 the Tang dynasty issued an edict which forced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uyghur_people" title="Uyghur people">Uighurs</a> in the capital, Chang'an, to wear their ethnic dress, stopped them from marrying Chinese females, and banned them from passing off as Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198522_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198522-227">[224]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jingyun_Bell.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2272" data-file-width="1704" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Jingyun_Bell.JPG/220px-Jingyun_Bell.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Jingyun_Bell.JPG/330px-Jingyun_Bell.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Jingyun_Bell.JPG/440px-Jingyun_Bell.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jingyun_Bell.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The bronze Jingyun Bell cast 711, height 247 cm high, weight 6,500 kg, now in the Xi'an Bell Tower</div></div></div>
<p>Chang'an was the center of the central government, the home of the imperial family, and was filled with splendor and wealth. However, incidentally it was not the economic hub during the Tang dynasty. The city of <a href="/wiki/Yangzhou" title="Yangzhou">Yangzhou</a> along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_of_China" title="Grand Canal of China">Grand Canal</a> and close to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a> was the greatest economic center during the Tang era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200246_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200246-199">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198517–18_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198517–18-228">[225]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yangzhou was the headquarters for the Tang government's salt monopoly, and was the greatest industrial center of China. It acted as a midpoint in shipping of foreign goods that would be organized and distributed to the major cities of the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200246_199-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200246-199">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198517–18_228-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198517–18-228">[225]</a></sup> Much like the seaport of <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> in the south, Yangzhou boasted thousands of foreign traders from all across Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198517–18_228-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198517–18-228">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReischauer1940143–144_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReischauer1940143–144-229">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>There was also the secondary capital city of <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a>, which was the favored capital of the two by <a href="/wiki/Wu_Zetian" title="Wu Zetian">Empress Wu</a>. In the year 691 she had more than 100,000 families (more than 500,000 people) from around the region of Chang'an move to populate Luoyang instead. With a population of about a million, Luoyang became the second largest city in the empire, and with its close proximity to the Luo River it benefited from southern agricultural fertility and trade traffic of the Grand Canal. However, the Tang court eventually demoted its capital status and did not visit Luoyang after the year 743, when Chang'an's problem of acquiring adequate supplies and stores for the year was solved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200246_199-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200246-199">[196]</a></sup> As early as 736, granaries were built at critical points along the route from Yangzhou to Chang'an, which eliminated shipment delays, spoilage, and pilfering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198518–19_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198518–19-230">[227]</a></sup> An artificial lake used as a transshipment pool was dredged east of Chang'an in 743, where curious northerners could finally see the array of boats found in southern China, delivering tax and tribute items to the imperial court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198519–20_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198519–20-231">[228]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_literature" title="Chinese literature">Chinese literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tang_poetry" title="Tang poetry">Tang poetry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%22Lan-ting_Xu%22_Preface_to_the_Poems_Composed_at_the_Orchid_Pavilion,_copy_by_an_artist_in_the_Tang_dynasty_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1560" data-file-width="4673" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/%22Lan-ting_Xu%22_Preface_to_the_Poems_Composed_at_the_Orchid_Pavilion%2C_copy_by_an_artist_in_the_Tang_dynasty_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/400px-%22Lan-ting_Xu%22_Preface_to_the_Poems_Composed_at_the_Orchid_Pavilion%2C_copy_by_an_artist_in_the_Tang_dynasty_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/%22Lan-ting_Xu%22_Preface_to_the_Poems_Composed_at_the_Orchid_Pavilion%2C_copy_by_an_artist_in_the_Tang_dynasty_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/600px-%22Lan-ting_Xu%22_Preface_to_the_Poems_Composed_at_the_Orchid_Pavilion%2C_copy_by_an_artist_in_the_Tang_dynasty_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/%22Lan-ting_Xu%22_Preface_to_the_Poems_Composed_at_the_Orchid_Pavilion%2C_copy_by_an_artist_in_the_Tang_dynasty_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/800px-%22Lan-ting_Xu%22_Preface_to_the_Poems_Composed_at_the_Orchid_Pavilion%2C_copy_by_an_artist_in_the_Tang_dynasty_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%22Lan-ting_Xu%22_Preface_to_the_Poems_Composed_at_the_Orchid_Pavilion,_copy_by_an_artist_in_the_Tang_dynasty_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Tang dynasty era copy of the preface to the <i><a href="/wiki/Lantingji_Xu" title="Lantingji Xu">Lantingji Xu</a></i> poems composed at the <a href="/wiki/Orchid_Pavilion_Gathering" title="Orchid Pavilion Gathering">Orchid Pavilion Gathering</a>, originally attributed to <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xizhi" title="Wang Xizhi">Wang Xizhi</a> (303–361 AD) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(265-420)" title="Jin dynasty (265-420)">Jin dynasty</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LiBai-Kalligraphie.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="472" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/LiBai-Kalligraphie.jpg/300px-LiBai-Kalligraphie.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/LiBai-Kalligraphie.jpg/450px-LiBai-Kalligraphie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/72/LiBai-Kalligraphie.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LiBai-Kalligraphie.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A poem by <a href="/wiki/Li_Bai" title="Li Bai">Li Bai</a> (701–762 AD), the only surviving example of Li Bai's <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">calligraphy</a>, housed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collections_of_the_Palace_Museum" title="Collections of the Palace Museum">Palace Museum</a> in Beijing.</div></div></div>
<p>The Tang period was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_age" title="Golden age">golden age</a> of Chinese literature and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_art" title="Chinese art">art</a>. Over 48,900 poems penned by some 2,200 Tang authors have survived to the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999120_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999120-232">[229]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarper200533_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarper200533-233">[230]</a></sup> Skill in the composition of poetry became a required study for those wishing to pass imperial examinations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002259_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002259-234">[231]</a></sup> while poetry was also heavily competitive; poetry contests amongst guests at banquets and courtiers were common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002137_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002137-235">[232]</a></sup> Poetry styles that were popular in the Tang included <a href="/wiki/Shi_(poetry)#Gushi" title="Shi (poetry)"><i>gushi</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/Shi_(poetry)#Jintishi" title="Shi (poetry)"><i>jintishi</i></a>, with the renowned poet <a href="/wiki/Li_Bai" title="Li Bai">Li Bai</a> (701–762) famous for the former style, and poets like <a href="/wiki/Wang_Wei_(Tang_dynasty)" title="Wang Wei (Tang dynasty)">Wang Wei</a> (701–761) and <a href="/wiki/Cui_Hao_(poet)" title="Cui Hao (poet)">Cui Hao</a> (704–754) famous for their use of the latter. <i>Jintishi</i> poetry, or regulated verse, is in the form of eight-line <a href="/wiki/Stanza" title="Stanza">stanzas</a> or seven <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">characters</a> per line with a fixed pattern of tones that required the second and third couplets to be antithetical (although the <a href="/wiki/Antithesis" title="Antithesis">antithesis</a> is often lost in translation to other languages).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006102_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006102-236">[233]</a></sup> Tang poems remained popular and great emulation of Tang era poetry began in the Song dynasty; in that period, Yan Yu (嚴羽; active 1194–1245) was the first to confer the poetry of the High Tang (c. 713–766) era with "canonical status within the classical poetic tradition." Yan Yu reserved the position of highest esteem among all Tang poets for <a href="/wiki/Du_Fu" title="Du Fu">Du Fu</a> (712–770), who was not viewed as such in his own era, and was branded by his peers as an anti-traditional rebel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYu199875–76_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYu199875–76-237">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Classical_Prose_Movement" title="Classical Prose Movement">Classical Prose Movement</a> was spurred in large part by the writings of Tang authors <a href="/wiki/Liu_Zongyuan" title="Liu Zongyuan">Liu Zongyuan</a> (773–819) and <a href="/wiki/Han_Yu" title="Han Yu">Han Yu</a> (768–824). This new prose style broke away from the poetry tradition of the <i>piantiwen</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">騙體文</span></span>, "parallel prose") style begun in the Han dynasty. Although writers of the Classical Prose Movement imitated <i>piantiwen</i>, they criticized it for its often vague content and lack of colloquial language, focusing more on clarity and precision to make their writing more direct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006106_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006106-238">[235]</a></sup> This <i>guwen</i> (archaic prose) style can be traced back to Han Yu, and would become largely associated with <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">orthodox</a> <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuters198752_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuters198752-239">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>Short story fiction and tales were also popular during the Tang, one of the more famous ones being <i>Yingying's Biography</i> by <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Zhen" title="Yuan Zhen">Yuan Zhen</a> (779–831), which was widely circulated in his own time and by the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> (1279–1368) became the basis for plays in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_opera" title="Chinese opera">Chinese opera</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006104–105_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006104–105-240">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWong197997_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWong197997-241">[238]</a></sup> Timothy C. Wong places this story within the wider context of Tang love tales, which often share the plot designs of quick passion, inescapable societal pressure leading to the abandonment of romance, followed by a period of <a href="/wiki/Melancholia" title="Melancholia">melancholy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWong197995–100_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWong197995–100-242">[239]</a></sup> Wong states that this scheme lacks the undying vows and total self-commitment to love found in Western romances such as <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet" title="Romeo and Juliet">Romeo and Juliet</a></i>, but that underlying traditional Chinese values of inseparableness of self from one's environment (including human society) served to create the necessary fictional device of romantic tension.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWong197998–99_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWong197998–99-243">[240]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Li_Shiming_Fountain_Memory.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="377" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Li_Shiming_Fountain_Memory.jpg/170px-Li_Shiming_Fountain_Memory.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Li_Shiming_Fountain_Memory.jpg/255px-Li_Shiming_Fountain_Memory.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Li_Shiming_Fountain_Memory.jpg/340px-Li_Shiming_Fountain_Memory.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Li_Shiming_Fountain_Memory.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">Calligraphy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong</a> on a Tang <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a></div></div></div>
<p>There were large <a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia" title="Encyclopedia">encyclopedias</a> published in the Tang. The <i><a href="/wiki/Yiwen_Leiju" title="Yiwen Leiju">Yiwen Leiju</a></i> encyclopedia was compiled in 624 by the chief editor <a href="/wiki/Ouyang_Xun" title="Ouyang Xun">Ouyang Xun</a> (557–641) as well as <a href="/wiki/Linghu_Defen" title="Linghu Defen">Linghu Defen</a> (582–666) and <a href="/wiki/Chen_Shuda" title="Chen Shuda">Chen Shuda</a> (d. 635). The encyclopedia <i><a href="/wiki/Treatise_on_Astrology_of_the_Kaiyuan_Era" title="Treatise on Astrology of the Kaiyuan Era">Treatise on Astrology of the Kaiyuan Era</a></i> was fully compiled in 729 by <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Siddha" title="Gautama Siddha">Gautama Siddha</a> (fl. 8th century), an ethnic Indian astronomer, astrologer, and scholar born in the capital Chang'an.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/History_of_geography#China" title="History of geography">Chinese geographers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Jia_Dan" title="Jia Dan">Jia Dan</a> wrote accurate descriptions of places far abroad. In his work written between 785 and 805, he described the sea route going into the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, and that the medieval <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranians</a> (whom he called the people of Luo-He-Yi) had erected 'ornamental pillars' in the sea that acted as <a href="/wiki/Lighthouse" title="Lighthouse">lighthouse</a> beacons for ships that might go astray.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c661_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c661-244">[241]</a></sup> Confirming Jia's reports about lighthouses in the Persian Gulf, Arabic writers a century after Jia wrote of the same structures, writers such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Mas%27udi" title="Al-Mas'udi">al-Mas'udi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Muqaddasi" title="Al-Muqaddasi">al-Muqaddasi</a>. The Tang dynasty Chinese diplomat <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xuance" title="Wang Xuance">Wang Xuance</a> traveled to <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a> (modern northeastern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>) during the 7th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESen20039,_22–24_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESen20039,_22–24-245">[242]</a></sup> Afterwards he wrote the book <i>Zhang Tianzhu Guotu</i> (Illustrated Accounts of Central India), which included a wealth of geographical information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a511_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a511-246">[243]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many histories of previous dynasties were compiled between 636 and 659 by court officials during and shortly after the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong of Tang</a>. These included the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Liang" title="Book of Liang">Book of Liang</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Chen" title="Book of Chen">Book of Chen</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Northern_Qi" title="Book of Northern Qi">Book of Northern Qi</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Zhou" title="Book of Zhou">Book of Zhou</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Sui" title="Book of Sui">Book of Sui</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jin" title="Book of Jin">Book of Jin</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Northern_Dynasties" title="History of Northern Dynasties">History of Northern Dynasties</a></i> and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Southern_Dynasties" title="History of Southern Dynasties">History of Southern Dynasties</a></i>. Although not included in the official <i><a href="/wiki/Twenty-Four_Histories" title="Twenty-Four Histories">Twenty-Four Histories</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Tongdian" title="Tongdian">Tongdian</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tang_Huiyao" title="Tang Huiyao">Tang Huiyao</a></i> were nonetheless valuable written historical works of the Tang period. The <i><a href="/wiki/Shitong" title="Shitong">Shitong</a></i> written by <a href="/wiki/Liu_Zhiji" title="Liu Zhiji">Liu Zhiji</a> in 710 was a meta-history, as it covered the history of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_historiography" title="Chinese historiography">Chinese historiography</a> in past centuries until his time. The <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Tang_Records_on_the_Western_Regions" title="Great Tang Records on the Western Regions">Great Tang Records on the Western Regions</a></i>, compiled by <a href="/wiki/Bianji" title="Bianji">Bianji</a>, recounted the journey of <a href="/wiki/Xuanzang" title="Xuanzang">Xuanzang</a>, the Tang era's most renowned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist_monk" title="Buddhist monk">Buddhist monk</a>.
</p><p>Other important literary offerings included <a href="/wiki/Duan_Chengshi" title="Duan Chengshi">Duan Chengshi</a>'s (d. 863) <i><a href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Morsels_from_Youyang" title="Miscellaneous Morsels from Youyang">Miscellaneous Morsels from Youyang</a></i>, an entertaining collection of foreign legends and hearsay, reports on natural phenomena, short <a href="/wiki/Anecdote" title="Anecdote">anecdotes</a>, mythical and mundane tales, as well as notes on various subjects. The exact literary category or classification that Duan's large informal narrative would fit into is still debated amongst scholars and historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReed2003121_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReed2003121-247">[244]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion_and_philosophy">Religion and philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Religion and philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_China" title="Religion in China">Religion in China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosophy</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Anti-Buddhist_Persecution" title="Great Anti-Buddhist Persecution">Great Anti-Buddhist Persecution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TangBodhisattva.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="743" data-file-width="531" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/TangBodhisattva.JPG/170px-TangBodhisattva.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/TangBodhisattva.JPG/255px-TangBodhisattva.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/TangBodhisattva.JPG/340px-TangBodhisattva.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TangBodhisattva.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Tang dynasty sculpture of a <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">Bodhisattva</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anonymous-Describing_the_Doctrine_Under_a_Tree.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3027" data-file-width="2204" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Anonymous-Describing_the_Doctrine_Under_a_Tree.jpg/170px-Anonymous-Describing_the_Doctrine_Under_a_Tree.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Anonymous-Describing_the_Doctrine_Under_a_Tree.jpg/255px-Anonymous-Describing_the_Doctrine_Under_a_Tree.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Anonymous-Describing_the_Doctrine_Under_a_Tree.jpg/340px-Anonymous-Describing_the_Doctrine_Under_a_Tree.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anonymous-Describing_the_Doctrine_Under_a_Tree.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 8th-century silk wall scroll from <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang" title="Dunhuang">Dunhuang</a>, showing the <a href="/wiki/Pure_Land_Buddhism" title="Pure Land Buddhism">paradise of Amitabha</a></div></div></div>
<p>Since ancient times, some Chinese had believed in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">folk religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> that incorporated many deities. Practitioners believed the <a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">Tao</a> and the afterlife was a reality parallel to the living world, complete with its own bureaucracy and afterlife currency needed by dead ancestors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitfield2004333_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitfield2004333-248">[245]</a></sup> Funerary practices included providing the deceased with everything they might need in the afterlife, including animals, servants, entertainers, hunters, homes, and officials. This ideal is reflected in <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty_art" title="Tang dynasty art">Tang dynasty art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[246]</a></sup> This is also reflected in many short stories written in the Tang about people accidentally winding up in the realm of the dead, only to come back and report their experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitfield2004333_248-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitfield2004333-248">[245]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, originating in India around the time of <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a>, continued its influence during the Tang period and was accepted by some members of imperial family, becoming thoroughly sinicized and a permanent part of Chinese traditional culture. In an age before <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a> and figures such as <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> (1130–1200), Buddhism had begun to flourish in China during the <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">Northern and Southern dynasties</a>, and became the dominant ideology during the prosperous Tang. Buddhist monasteries played an integral role in Chinese society, offering lodging for travelers in remote areas, schools for children throughout the country, and a place for urban literati to stage social events and gatherings such as going-away parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999121_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999121-250">[247]</a></sup> Buddhist monasteries were also engaged in the economy, since their land property and serfs gave them enough revenues to set up mills, oil presses, and other enterprises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999122_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999122-251">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005181_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEberhard2005181-252">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200486_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200486-253">[250]</a></sup> Although the monasteries retained 'serfs', these monastery dependents could actually own property and employ others to help them in their work, including their own slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999126_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999126-254">[251]</a></sup>
</p><p>The prominent status of Buddhism in Chinese culture began to decline as the dynasty and central government declined as well during the late 8th century to 9th century. Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Convent" title="Convent">convents</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Buddhist_temples" title="List of Buddhist temples">temples</a> that were exempt from state taxes beforehand were targeted by the state for taxation. In 845 <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wuzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Wuzong of Tang">Emperor Wuzong of Tang</a> finally shut down 4,600 Buddhist monasteries along with 40,000 temples and shrines, forcing 260,000 Buddhist monks and nuns to return to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secular" title="Secular">secular</a> life;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200696_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200696-255">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200686_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200686-256">[253]</a></sup> this episode would later be dubbed one of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Buddhist_Persecutions_in_China" title="Four Buddhist Persecutions in China">Four Buddhist Persecutions in China</a>. Although the ban would be lifted just a few years after, Buddhism never regained its once dominant status in Chinese culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200696_255-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200696-255">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200686_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200686-256">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999124_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999124-257">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarper200534_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarper200534-258">[255]</a></sup> This situation also came about through a revival of interest in native Chinese philosophies such as Confucianism and Taoism. <a href="/wiki/Han_Yu" title="Han Yu">Han Yu</a> (786–824)—who <a href="/wiki/Arthur_F._Wright" title="Arthur F. Wright">Arthur F. Wright</a> stated was a "brilliant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polemicist" title="Polemicist">polemicist</a> and ardent <a href="/wiki/Xenophobia" title="Xenophobia">xenophobe</a>"—was one of the first men of the Tang to denounce Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright195988_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright195988-259">[256]</a></sup> Although his contemporaries found him crude and obnoxious, he would foreshadow the later persecution of Buddhism in the Tang, as well as the revival of Confucian theory with the rise of Neo-Confucianism of the Song dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright195988_259-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright195988-259">[256]</a></sup> Nonetheless, <a href="/wiki/Zen" title="Zen">Chán Buddhism</a> gained popularity amongst the educated elite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200696_255-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200696-255">[252]</a></sup> There were also many famous Chan monks from the Tang era, such as <a href="/wiki/Mazu_Daoyi" title="Mazu Daoyi">Mazu Daoyi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baizhang" title="Baizhang">Baizhang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Huangbo_Xiyun" title="Huangbo Xiyun">Huangbo Xiyun</a>. The sect of <a href="/wiki/Pure_Land_Buddhism" title="Pure Land Buddhism">Pure Land Buddhism</a> initiated by the Chinese monk <a href="/wiki/Huiyuan_(Buddhist)" title="Huiyuan (Buddhist)">Huiyuan</a> (334–416) was also just as popular as Chan Buddhism during the Tang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999123_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999123-260">[257]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wutai_Foguang_Si_2013.08.28_11-20-48.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Wutai_Foguang_Si_2013.08.28_11-20-48.jpg/220px-Wutai_Foguang_Si_2013.08.28_11-20-48.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Wutai_Foguang_Si_2013.08.28_11-20-48.jpg/330px-Wutai_Foguang_Si_2013.08.28_11-20-48.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Wutai_Foguang_Si_2013.08.28_11-20-48.jpg/440px-Wutai_Foguang_Si_2013.08.28_11-20-48.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wutai_Foguang_Si_2013.08.28_11-20-48.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A timber hall built in 857,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteinhardt2004228–229_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteinhardt2004228–229-261">[258]</a></sup> located at the Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Foguang_Temple" title="Foguang Temple">Foguang Temple</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wutai" title="Mount Wutai">Mount Wutai</a>, Shanxi</div></div></div>
<p>Rivaling Buddhism was Taoism, a native Chinese philosophical and religious belief system that found its roots in the <i><a href="/wiki/Tao_Te_Ching" title="Tao Te Ching">Tao Te Ching</a></i> (a text attributed to a 6th-century BC figure named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lao_Tzu" title="Lao Tzu">Lao Tzu</a>) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)">Zhuangzi</a></i>. The ruling Li family of the Tang dynasty actually claimed descent from the ancient Lao Tzu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200260_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200260-262">[259]</a></sup> On numerous occasions where Tang princes would become crown prince or Tang princesses taking vows as Taoist priestesses, their lavish former mansions would be converted into Taoist <a href="/wiki/Abbey" title="Abbey">abbeys</a> and places of worship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200260_262-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200260-262">[259]</a></sup> Many Taoists were associated with <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a> in their pursuits to find an <a href="/wiki/Elixir_of_life" title="Elixir of life">elixir of immortality</a> and a means to create gold from concocted mixtures of many other elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200681_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200681-263">[260]</a></sup> Although they never achieved their goals in either of these futile pursuits, they did contribute to the discovery of new metal alloys, porcelain products, and new dyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200681_263-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200681-263">[260]</a></sup> The historian Joseph Needham labeled the work of the Taoist alchemists as "protoscience rather than pseudoscience."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200681_263-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200681-263">[260]</a></sup> However, the close connection between Taoism and alchemy, which some <a href="/wiki/Sinology" title="Sinology">sinologists</a> have asserted, is refuted by <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Sivin" title="Nathan Sivin">Nathan Sivin</a>, who states that alchemy was just as prominent (if not more so) in the secular sphere and practiced more often by laymen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[261]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" Christianity</a></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Church_of_the_East_in_the_Middle_Ages.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="645" data-file-width="934" decoding="async" height="173"
