srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Church_of_the_East_in_the_Middle_Ages.svg/375px-Church_of_the_East_in_the_Middle_Ages.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Church_of_the_East_in_the_Middle_Ages.svg/500px-Church_of_the_East_in_the_Middle_Ages.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> and its largest extent during the Middle Ages.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The Tang dynasty also officially recognized various foreign religions. The <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a>, otherwise known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_Church" title="Nestorian Church">Nestorian Church</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East_in_China" title="Church of the East in China">Church of the East in China</a>, was given recognition by the Tang court. In 781, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_Stele" title="Nestorian Stele">Nestorian Stele</a> was created in order to honor the achievements of their community in China. A Christian monastery was established in Shaanxi province where the <a href="/wiki/Daqin_Pagoda" title="Daqin Pagoda">Daqin Pagoda</a> still stands, and inside the pagoda there is Christian-themed artwork. Although the religion largely died out after the Tang, it was revived in China following the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_conquest_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Mongol conquest of the Song dynasty">Mongol invasions</a> of the 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962215_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962215-265">[262]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdians</a> had been responsible for transmitting Buddhism to China from India during the 2nd to 4th centuries, soon afterwards they largely converted to <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> due to their links to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid" title="Sassanid">Sassanid</a> Persia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiu2001168_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiu2001168-266">[263]</a></sup> Sogdian merchants and their families living in cities such as Chang'an, Luoyang, and Xiangyang usually built a Zoroastrian temple once their local communities grew larger than 100 households.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoward2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6QPWXrCCzBIClpgPP1pgPA134_134]_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoward2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6QPWXrCCzBIClpgPP1pgPA134_134]-267">[264]</a></sup> Sogdians were also responsible for spreading <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manicheism" title="Manicheism">Manicheism</a> in Tang China and the <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_Khaganate" title="Uyghur Khaganate">Uighur Khaganate</a>. The Uighurs built the first Manichean monastery in China in 768, yet in 843 the Tang government ordered that the property of all Manichean monasteries be confiscated in response to the outbreak of war with the Uighurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiu2001168–69_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiu2001168–69-268">[265]</a></sup> With the blanket ban on foreign religions two years later, Manicheism was driven underground and never flourished in China again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiu2001169_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiu2001169-269">[266]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leisure">Leisure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Leisure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:149px;max-width:149px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:122px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palefrenier_menant_deux_chevaux_par_Han_Gan.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1636" data-file-width="1959" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Palefrenier_menant_deux_chevaux_par_Han_Gan.jpg/147px-Palefrenier_menant_deux_chevaux_par_Han_Gan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Palefrenier_menant_deux_chevaux_par_Han_Gan.jpg/221px-Palefrenier_menant_deux_chevaux_par_Han_Gan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Palefrenier_menant_deux_chevaux_par_Han_Gan.jpg/294px-Palefrenier_menant_deux_chevaux_par_Han_Gan.jpg 2x" width="147"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><i>A Man Herding Horses</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Han_Gan" title="Han Gan">Han Gan</a> (706–783), a court artist under Xuanzong</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:239px;max-width:239px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:122px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spring_Outing_of_the_Tang_Court.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="521" data-file-width="1006" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Spring_Outing_of_the_Tang_Court.jpg/237px-Spring_Outing_of_the_Tang_Court.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Spring_Outing_of_the_Tang_Court.jpg/356px-Spring_Outing_of_the_Tang_Court.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Spring_Outing_of_the_Tang_Court.jpg/474px-Spring_Outing_of_the_Tang_Court.jpg 2x" width="237"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><i>Spring Outing of the Tang Court</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Xuan" title="Zhang Xuan">Zhang Xuan</a> (713–755)</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Much more than earlier periods, the Tang era was renowned for the time reserved for leisure activity, especially for those in the upper classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002149_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002149-270">[267]</a></sup> Many outdoor sports and activities were enjoyed during the Tang, including <a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">archery</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200239,_170_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200239,_170-271">[268]</a></sup> hunting,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200222,_32_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200222,_32-272">[269]</a></sup> horse <a href="/wiki/Polo" title="Polo">polo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200216,_90_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200216,_90-273">[270]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cuju" title="Cuju">cuju</a> (soccer),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002151–152_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002151–152-274">[271]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cockfighting" title="Cockfighting">cockfighting</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002173–174_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002173–174-275">[272]</a></sup> and even <a href="/wiki/Tug_of_war" title="Tug of war">tug of war</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002152_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002152-276">[273]</a></sup> Government officials were granted <a href="/wiki/Annual_leave" title="Annual leave">vacations</a> during their tenure in office. Officials were granted 30 days off every three years to visit their parents if they lived 1,000 mi (1,600 km) away, or 15 days off if the parents lived more than 167 mi (269 km) away (travel time not included).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002149_270-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002149-270">[267]</a></sup> Officials were granted nine days of vacation time for weddings of a son or daughter, and either five, three, or one days/day off for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuptial" title="Nuptial">nuptials</a> of close relatives (travel time not included).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002149_270-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002149-270">[267]</a></sup> Officials also received a total of three days off for their son's capping initiation rite into manhood, and one day off for the ceremony of initiation rite of a close relative's son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002149_270-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002149-270">[267]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tang_Dynasty-Polo_Game_Relief_Block.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="823" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Tang_Dynasty-Polo_Game_Relief_Block.JPG/220px-Tang_Dynasty-Polo_Game_Relief_Block.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Tang_Dynasty-Polo_Game_Relief_Block.JPG/330px-Tang_Dynasty-Polo_Game_Relief_Block.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Tang_Dynasty-Polo_Game_Relief_Block.JPG/440px-Tang_Dynasty-Polo_Game_Relief_Block.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tang_Dynasty-Polo_Game_Relief_Block.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Tang <a href="/wiki/Sancai" title="Sancai">sancai</a>-glazed carved relief showing horseback riders playing <a href="/wiki/Polo" title="Polo">polo</a></div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_holidays" title="Traditional Chinese holidays">Traditional Chinese holidays</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Chinese_New_Year" title="Chinese New Year">Chinese New Year</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lantern_Festival" title="Lantern Festival">Lantern Festival</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cold_Food_Festival" title="Cold Food Festival">Cold Food Festival</a>, and others were universal holidays. In the capital city of <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a> there was always lively celebration, especially for the <a href="/wiki/Lantern_Festival" title="Lantern Festival">Lantern Festival</a> since the city's nighttime <a href="/wiki/Curfew" title="Curfew">curfew</a> was lifted by the government for three days straight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002150–154_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002150–154-277">[274]</a></sup> Between the years 628 and 758, the imperial throne bestowed a total of sixty-nine grand <a href="/wiki/Carnival" title="Carnival">carnivals</a> nationwide, granted by the emperor in the case of special circumstances such as important military victories, abundant <a href="/wiki/Harvest" title="Harvest">harvests</a> after a long <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a> or <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>, the granting of <a href="/wiki/Amnesty" title="Amnesty">amnesties</a>, the installment of a new <a href="/wiki/Crown_prince" title="Crown prince">crown prince</a>, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002154–155_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002154–155-278">[275]</a></sup> For special celebration in the Tang era, lavish and gargantuan-sized feasts were sometimes prepared, as the imperial court had staffed agencies to prepare the meals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002132_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002132-279">[276]</a></sup> This included a prepared feast for 1,100 elders of Chang'an in 664, a feast for 3,500 officers of the Divine Strategy Army in 768, and a feast for 1,200 women of the palace and members of the imperial family in the year 826.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002132_279-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002132-279">[276]</a></sup> Drinking wine and alcoholic beverages was heavily ingrained into Chinese culture, as people drank for nearly every social event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002142–147_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002142–147-280">[277]</a></sup> A court official in the 8th century allegedly had a serpentine-shaped structure called the 'Ale Grotto' built with 50,000 bricks on the groundfloor that each featured a bowl from which his friends could drink.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002143_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002143-281">[278]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_in_clothing">Status in clothing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Status in clothing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tang-4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2751" data-file-width="1847" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Tang-4.jpg/170px-Tang-4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Tang-4.jpg/255px-Tang-4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Tang-4.jpg/340px-Tang-4.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tang-4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A late Tang or early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Dynasties" title="Five Dynasties">Five Dynasties</a> era <a href="/wiki/Silk_painting" title="Silk painting">silk painting</a> on a banner depicting <a href="/wiki/Guanyin" title="Guanyin">Guanyin</a> and a female attendant in silk robes, from the <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang" title="Dunhuang">Dunhuang</a> caves, now in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>In general, garments were made from silk, wool, or linen depending on your social status and what you could afford. Furthermore, there were laws that specified what kinds of clothing could be worn by whom. The color of the clothing also indicated rank. During this period, China's power, culture, economy, and influence were thriving. As a result, women could afford to wear loose-fitting, wide-sleeved garments. Even lower-class women's robes would have sleeves four to five feet in width.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[279]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Position_of_women">Position of women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Position of women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_ancient_and_imperial_China#Tang_dynasty" title="Women in ancient and imperial China">Women in ancient and imperial China § Tang dynasty</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:394px;max-width:394px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chou_Fang_004.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="460" data-file-width="624" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Chou_Fang_004.jpg/250px-Chou_Fang_004.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Chou_Fang_004.jpg/375px-Chou_Fang_004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Chou_Fang_004.jpg/500px-Chou_Fang_004.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><i>Beauties Wearing Flowers</i> by <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Fang_(Tang_dynasty)" title="Zhou Fang (Tang dynasty)">Zhou Fang</a>, 8th century</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:138px;max-width:138px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joueuse_de_polo_Tang_29102.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1701" data-file-width="1266" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Joueuse_de_polo_Tang_29102.jpg/136px-Joueuse_de_polo_Tang_29102.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Joueuse_de_polo_Tang_29102.jpg/204px-Joueuse_de_polo_Tang_29102.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Joueuse_de_polo_Tang_29102.jpg/272px-Joueuse_de_polo_Tang_29102.jpg 2x" width="136"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Woman playing polo, 8th century</div></div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Li_Xian%27s_tomb,_palace_ladies.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3065" data-file-width="2887" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Li_Xian%27s_tomb%2C_palace_ladies.jpg/220px-Li_Xian%27s_tomb%2C_palace_ladies.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Li_Xian%27s_tomb%2C_palace_ladies.jpg/330px-Li_Xian%27s_tomb%2C_palace_ladies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Li_Xian%27s_tomb%2C_palace_ladies.jpg/440px-Li_Xian%27s_tomb%2C_palace_ladies.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Li_Xian%27s_tomb,_palace_ladies.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Palace ladies in a garden from a mural of Prince <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li_Xian_(prince)" title="Li Xian (prince)">Li Xian</a>'s tomb in the <a href="/wiki/Qianling_Mausoleum" title="Qianling Mausoleum">Qianling Mausoleum</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Wu_Zetian" title="Wu Zetian">Wu Zetian</a> was also buried in 706</div></div></div>
<p>Concepts of women's social rights and social status during the Tang era were notably liberal-minded for the period. However, this was largely reserved for urban women of elite status, as men and women in the rural countryside labored hard in their different set of tasks; with wives and daughters responsible for more domestic tasks of weaving textiles and rearing of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silk_worm" title="Silk worm">silk worms</a>, while men tended to farming in the fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200232_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200232-116">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>There were many women in the Tang era who gained access to religious authority by taking vows as Taoist priestesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200260_262-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200260-262">[259]</a></sup> The head mistresses of high-class <a href="/wiki/Courtesan" title="Courtesan">courtesans</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an#East_Central_Chang'an" title="Chang'an">North Hamlet</a> of the capital Chang'an acquired large amounts of wealth and power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200264–66_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200264–66-283">[280]</a></sup> Such <a href="/wiki/Courtesan" title="Courtesan">courtesans</a>, who likely influenced the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Geisha" title="Geisha">geishas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200264_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200264-284">[281]</a></sup> were well respected. These courtesans were known as great singers and poets, supervised banquets and feasts, knew the rules to all the <a href="/wiki/Drinking_game" title="Drinking game">drinking games</a>, and were trained to have the utmost respectable <a href="/wiki/Table_manners" title="Table manners">table manners</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200264–66_283-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200264–66-283">[280]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although they were renowned for their polite behavior, the courtesans were known to dominate the conversation among elite men, and were not afraid to openly castigate or criticize prominent male guests who talked too much or too loudly, boasted too much of their accomplishments, or had in some way ruined dinner for everyone by rude behavior (on one occasion a courtesan even beat up a drunken man who had insulted her).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn200266_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn200266-285">[282]</a></sup> When singing to entertain guests, courtesans not only composed the lyrics to their own songs, but they popularized a new form of lyrical verse by singing lines written by various renowned and famous men in Chinese history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999120_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999120-232">[229]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was fashionable for women to be full-figured (or plump). Men enjoyed the presence of assertive, active women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999114–115_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999114–115-286">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962165–166_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962165–166-287">[284]</a></sup> The foreign horse-riding sport of <a href="/wiki/Polo" title="Polo">polo</a> from Persia became a wildly popular trend among the Chinese elite, and women often played the sport (as glazed <a href="/wiki/Earthenware" title="Earthenware">earthenware</a> figurines from the time period portray).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999114–115_286-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999114–115-286">[283]</a></sup> The preferred hairstyle for women was to bunch their hair up like "an elaborate edifice above the forehead",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962165–166_287-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962165–166-287">[284]</a></sup> while affluent ladies wore extravagant head ornaments, combs, pearl necklaces, face powders, and perfumes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGernet1962165_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGernet1962165-288">[285]</a></sup> A law was passed in 671 which attempted to force women to wear hats with veils again in order to promote decency, but these laws were ignored as some women started wearing caps and even no hats at all, as well as men's riding clothes and boots, and tight-sleeved bodices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer198528–29_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer198528–29-289">[286]</a></sup>
</p><p>There were some prominent court women after the era of <a href="/wiki/Wu_Zetian" title="Wu Zetian">Empress Wu</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Yang_Guifei" title="Yang Guifei">Yang Guifei</a> (719–756), who had Emperor Xuanzong appoint many of her relatives and cronies to important ministerial and martial positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200699_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200699-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the earlier <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">Northern and Southern dynasties</a> (420–589), and perhaps even earlier, the drinking of tea (<i><a href="/wiki/Camellia_sinensis" title="Camellia sinensis">Camellia sinensis</a></i>) became popular in southern China. Tea was viewed then as a beverage of tasteful pleasure and with pharmacological purpose as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999120_232-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999120-232">[229]</a></sup> During the Tang dynasty, tea became synonymous with everything sophisticated in society. The poet <a href="/wiki/Lu_Tong" title="Lu Tong">Lu Tong</a> (790–835) devoted most of his poetry to his love of tea. The 8th-century author <a href="/wiki/Lu_Yu" title="Lu Yu">Lu Yu</a> (known as the Sage of Tea) even wrote a treatise on the art of drinking tea, called <i><a href="/wiki/The_Classic_of_Tea" title="The Classic of Tea">The Classic of Tea</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey199995_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey199995-290">[287]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Gift_wrapping" title="Gift wrapping">wrapping paper</a> had been used in China since the 2nd century BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d122_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d122-291">[288]</a></sup> during the Tang dynasty the Chinese were using wrapping paper as folded and sewn square bags to hold and preserve the flavor of tea leaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d122_291-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d122-291">[288]</a></sup> This followed many other uses for paper such as the first recorded use of <a href="/wiki/Toilet_paper" title="Toilet paper">toilet paper</a> made in 589 by the scholar-official <a href="/wiki/Yan_Zhitui" title="Yan Zhitui">Yan Zhitui</a> (531–591),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d123_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d123-292">[289]</a></sup> confirmed in 851 by an Arab traveler who remarked that Tang Chinese lacked cleanliness because they relied on toilet paper instead of washing themselves with water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d123_292-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d123-292">[289]</a></sup>
</p><p>In ancient times, the Chinese had outlined the five most basic foodstuffs known as the five grains: <a href="/wiki/Sesamum" title="Sesamum">sesamum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legumes</a>, wheat, <a href="/wiki/Panicle" title="Panicle">panicled</a> <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutinous" title="Glutinous">glutinous</a> millet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESong19663–4_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESong19663–4-293">[290]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> <a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia" title="Encyclopedia">encyclopedist</a> <a href="/wiki/Song_Yingxing" title="Song Yingxing">Song Yingxing</a> (1587–1666) noted that rice was not counted amongst the five grains from the time of the legendary and deified Chinese sage <a href="/wiki/Shennong" title="Shennong">Shennong</a> (the existence of whom Yingxing wrote was "an uncertain matter") into the 2nd millenniums BC, because the properly wet and humid climate in southern China for growing rice was not yet fully settled or cultivated by the Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESong19663–4_293-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESong19663–4-293">[290]</a></sup> Song Yingxing also noted that in the Ming dynasty, seven tenths of civilians' food was rice. During the Tang dynasty rice was not only the most important staple in southern China, but had also become popular in the north where central authority resided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang_Saishi200318_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang_Saishi200318-294">[291]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Tang dynasty, wheat replaced the position of millet and became the main staple crop. As a consequence, wheat cake shared a considerable amount in the staple of Tang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang_Saishi20031_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang_Saishi20031-295">[292]</a></sup> There were four main kinds of cake: steamed cake, boiled cake, pancake, and Hu cake. Steamed cake was consumed commonly by both civilians and aristocrats. Like <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rougamo" title="Rougamo">rougamo</a></i> in modern Chinese cuisine, steamed cake was usually stuffed with meat and vegetables. Shops and packmen regularly sold inexpensive steamed cake on the streets of Chang’an.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li_1999_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li_1999-296">[293]</a></sup> Boiled cake was the staple of the Northern Dynasties, and it kept its popularity in the Tang dynasty. It included a wide variety of dishes similar to modern <a href="/wiki/Wonton" title="Wonton">wonton</a>, noodles, and many other kinds of food that soak wheat in water. While aristocrats favored wonton, civilians usually consumed noodles and noodle slice soup that were easier to produce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang_Saishi20036_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang_Saishi20036-297">[294]</a></sup> Pancakes was rare in China before the Tang, when it gained popularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[295]</a></sup> Food shops in Tang cities such as Chang'an commonly sold both pancakes and <a href="/wiki/Dumpling" title="Dumpling">dumplings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li_1999_296-1"><a href="#cite_note-Li_1999-296">[293]</a></sup> Hu cake, which means "foreign cake", was extremely popular during the Tang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang_Saishi20034_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang_Saishi20034-299">[296]</a></sup> Hu cake was toasted in the oven, covered with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sesame_seed" title="Sesame seed">sesame seeds</a>, and served at taverns, inns and shops. Japanese Buddhist monk Ennin observed that Hu cake was popular among all of China's civilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[297]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Tang, the many common foodstuffs and cooking ingredients in addition to those already listed were barley, garlic, salt, turnips, soybeans, pears, apricots, peaches, apples, pomegranates, jujubes, rhubarb, hazelnuts, pine nuts, chestnuts, walnuts, yams, taro, etc. The various meats that were consumed included pork, chicken, <a href="/wiki/Lamb_and_mutton" title="Lamb and mutton">lamb</a> (especially preferred in the north), <a href="/wiki/Sea_otter" title="Sea otter">sea otter</a>, bear (which was hard to catch, but there were recipes for steamed, boiled, and <a href="/wiki/Marination" title="Marination">marinated</a> bear), and even <a href="/wiki/Bactrian_camel" title="Bactrian camel">Bactrian camels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002120_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002120-301">[298]</a></sup> In the south along the coast meat from seafood was by default the most common, as the Chinese enjoyed eating cooked <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a> with <a href="/wiki/Cinnamon" title="Cinnamon">cinnamon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sichuan_pepper" title="Sichuan pepper">Sichuan pepper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardamom" title="Cardamom">cardamom</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ginger" title="Ginger">ginger</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Oyster" title="Oyster">oysters</a> with wine, fried <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a> with ginger and vinegar, <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_crab" title="Horseshoe crab">horseshoe crabs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portunus" title="Portunus">red swimming crabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tetraodontidae" title="Tetraodontidae">pufferfish</a>, which the Chinese called "river piglet".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002121_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002121-302">[299]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the trade overseas and over land, the Chinese acquired peaches from <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a>, date palms, pistachios, and figs from <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Greater Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pine_nut" title="Pine nut">pine nuts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ginseng" title="Ginseng">ginseng</a> roots from <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mango" title="Mango">mangoes</a> from <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002123_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002123-303">[300]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer19851–2_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer19851–2-304">[301]</a></sup> In China, there was a great demand for sugar; during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Harsha" title="Harsha">Harsha</a> over <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North India</a> (r. 606–647), Indian envoys to the Tang brought two makers of sugar who successfully taught the Chinese how to cultivate <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESen200338–40_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESen200338–40-305">[302]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200476,_83–84_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200476,_83–84-306">[303]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">Cotton</a> also came from India as a finished product from <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a>, although it was during the Tang that the Chinese began to grow and process cotton, and by the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> it became the prime textile fabric in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200483_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200483-307">[304]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some foods were also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_taboo" title="Food taboo">off-limits</a>, as the Tang court encouraged people <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beef_taboo#China" title="Beef taboo">not to eat beef</a>. This was due to the role of the bull as a valuable <a href="/wiki/Working_animal" title="Working animal">working animal</a>. From 831 to 833 <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wenzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Wenzong of Tang">Emperor Wenzong of Tang</a> even banned the slaughter of cattle on the grounds of his religious convictions to <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002125_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002125-308">[305]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methods of <a href="/wiki/Food_preservation" title="Food preservation">food preservation</a> were important, and practiced throughout China. The common people used simple methods of preservation, such as digging deep ditches and trenches, <a href="/wiki/Brining" title="Brining">brining</a>, and salting their foods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002126–127_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002126–127-309">[306]</a></sup> The emperor had large ice pits located in the parks in and around Chang'an for preserving food, while the wealthy and elite had their own smaller ice pits. Each year the emperor had laborers carve 1000 blocks of ice from frozen creeks in mountain valleys, each block with the dimension of 3 ft (0.91 m) by 3 ft by 3.5 ft (1.1 m). Frozen delicacies such as chilled melon were enjoyed during the summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002126_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002126-310">[307]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Science and technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_of_the_Tang_dynasty" title="Science and technology of the Tang dynasty">Science and technology of the Tang dynasty</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_and_technology_in_China" title="History of science and technology in China">History of science and technology in China</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_inventions" title="List of Chinese inventions">List of Chinese inventions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_discoveries" title="List of Chinese discoveries">List of Chinese discoveries</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engineering">Engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Square_mirror_with_phoenix_motif,_Tang_Dynasty.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Square_mirror_with_phoenix_motif%2C_Tang_Dynasty.jpg/220px-Square_mirror_with_phoenix_motif%2C_Tang_Dynasty.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Square_mirror_with_phoenix_motif%2C_Tang_Dynasty.jpg/330px-Square_mirror_with_phoenix_motif%2C_Tang_Dynasty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Square_mirror_with_phoenix_motif%2C_Tang_Dynasty.jpg/440px-Square_mirror_with_phoenix_motif%2C_Tang_Dynasty.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Square_mirror_with_phoenix_motif,_Tang_Dynasty.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A square <a href="/wiki/Bronze_mirror" title="Bronze mirror">bronze mirror</a> with <a href="/wiki/Fenghuang" title="Fenghuang">a phoenix</a> motif of gold and silver inlaid with <a href="/wiki/Lacquer" title="Lacquer">lacquer</a>, 8th-century</div></div></div>
<p>Technology during the Tang period was built also upon the precedents of the past. Previous advancements in clockworks and timekeeping included the mechanical gear systems of <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Heng" title="Zhang Heng">Zhang Heng</a> (78–139) and <a href="/wiki/Ma_Jun_(mechanical_engineer)" title="Ma Jun (mechanical engineer)">Ma Jun</a> (fl. 3rd century), which gave the Tang mathematician, mechanical engineer, astronomer, and monk <a href="/wiki/Yi_Xing" title="Yi Xing">Yi Xing</a> (683–727) inspiration when he invented the world's first clockwork <a href="/wiki/Escapement" title="Escapement">escapement</a> mechanism in 725.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a319_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a319-311">[308]</a></sup> This was used alongside a <a href="/wiki/Water_clock" title="Water clock">clepsydra</a> clock and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterwheel" title="Waterwheel">waterwheel</a> to power a rotating <a href="/wiki/Armillary_sphere" title="Armillary sphere">armillary sphere</a> in representation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical" title="Astronomical">astronomical</a> observation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b473–475_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b473–475-312">[309]</a></sup> Yi Xing's device also had a mechanically timed bell that was struck automatically every hour, and a drum that was struck automatically every quarter-hour; essentially, a <a href="/wiki/Striking_clock" title="Striking clock">striking clock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b473–474_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b473–474-313">[310]</a></sup> Yi Xing's <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_clock" title="Astronomical clock">astronomical clock</a> and water-powered armillary sphere became well known throughout the country, since students attempting to pass the imperial examinations by 730 had to write an essay on the device as an exam requirement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b475_314-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b475-314">[311]</a></sup> However, the most common type of public and palace timekeeping device was the inflow clepsydra. Its design was improved c. 610 by the Sui-dynasty engineers Geng Xun and Yuwen Kai. They provided a <a href="/wiki/Steelyard_balance" title="Steelyard balance">steelyard balance</a> that allowed seasonal adjustment in the <a href="/wiki/Pressure_head" title="Pressure head">pressure head</a> of the compensating tank and could then control the rate of flow for different lengths of day and night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b480_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b480-315">[312]</a></sup>
</p><p>There were many other mechanical inventions during the Tang era. These included a 3 ft (0.91 m) tall mechanical wine server of the early 8th century that was in the shape of an artificial mountain, carved out of iron and rested on a <a href="/wiki/Lacquer" title="Lacquer">lacquered</a>-wooden tortoise frame. This intricate device used a hydraulic pump that siphoned wine out of metal <a href="/wiki/Chinese_dragon" title="Chinese dragon">dragon</a>-headed faucets, as well as tilting bowls that were timed to dip wine down, by force of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> when filled, into an artificial lake that had intricate iron leaves popping up as trays for placing party treats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002144_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002144-316">[313]</a></sup> Furthermore, as the historian Charles Benn describes it:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Midway up the southern side of the mountain was a dragon<span class="nowrap"> </span>... the beast opened its mouth and spit brew into a goblet seated on a large [iron] lotus leaf beneath. When the cup was 80% full, the dragon ceased spewing ale, and a guest immediately seized the goblet. If he was slow in draining the cup and returning it to the leaf, the door of a pavilion at the top of the mountain opened and a mechanical wine server, dressed in a cap and gown, emerged with a wooden bat in his hand. As soon as the guest returned the goblet, the dragon refilled it, the wine server withdrew, and the doors of the pavilion closed<span class="nowrap"> </span>... A pump siphoned the ale that flowed into the ale pool through a hidden hole and returned the brew to the reservoir [holding more than 16 quarts/15 liters of wine] inside the mountain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002144_316-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002144-316">[313]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Yet the use of a teasing mechanical puppet in this wine-serving device wasn't exactly a novel invention of the Tang, since the use of mechanical puppets in China date back to the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> (221–207 BC). In the 3rd century <a href="/wiki/Ma_Jun_(mechanical_engineer)" title="Ma Jun (mechanical engineer)">Ma Jun</a> had an entire mechanical puppet theater operated by the rotation of a waterwheel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b158_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b158-317">[314]</a></sup> There was also an automatic wine-server known in the ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Roman" title="Greco-Roman">Greco-Roman</a> world, a design of the Greek inventor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heron_of_Alexandria" title="Heron of Alexandria">Heron of Alexandria</a> that employed an urn with an inner valve and a lever device similar to the one described above. There are many stories of <a href="/wiki/Automaton" title="Automaton">automatons</a> used in the Tang, including general Yang Wulian's wooden statue of a monk who stretched his hands out to collect contributions; when the number of coins reached a certain weight, the mechanical figure moved his arms to deposit them in a satchel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b163_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b163-318">[315]</a></sup> This weight-and-lever mechanism was exactly like Heron's penny <a href="/wiki/Slot_machine" title="Slot machine">slot machine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b163_footnote_c_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b163_footnote_c-319">[316]</a></sup> Other devices included one by Wang Ju, whose "wooden otter" could allegedly catch fish; Needham suspects a <a href="/wiki/Spring_(device)" title="Spring (device)">spring trap</a> of some kind was employed here.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b163_318-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b163-318">[315]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the realm of structural engineering and technical <a href="/wiki/Chinese_architecture" title="Chinese architecture">Chinese architecture</a>, there were also government standard building codes, outlined in the early Tang book of the <i>Yingshan Ling</i> (National Building Law).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuo19981_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuo19981-320">[317]</a></sup> Fragments of this book have survived in the <i>Tang Lü</i> (The Tang Code),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuo19983_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuo19983-321">[318]</a></sup> while the Song dynasty architectural manual of the <i><a href="/wiki/Yingzao_Fashi" title="Yingzao Fashi">Yingzao Fashi</a></i> (State Building Standards) by <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_the_Song_dynasty#Literature" title="Architecture of the Song dynasty">Li Jie</a> (1065–1101) in 1103 is the oldest existing technical treatise on Chinese architecture that has survived in full.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuo19981_320-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuo19981-320">[317]</a></sup> During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuanzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuanzong of Tang">Emperor Xuanzong of Tang</a> (712–756) there were 34,850 registered <a href="/wiki/Artisan" title="Artisan">craftsmen</a> serving the state, managed by the Agency of Palace Buildings (Jingzuo Jian).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuo19983_321-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuo19983-321">[318]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Woodblock_printing">Woodblock printing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Woodblock printing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">Woodblock printing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Playing_cards" title="Playing cards">Playing cards</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_playing_cards" title="Chinese playing cards">Chinese playing cards</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jingangjing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3438" data-file-width="4917" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Jingangjing.jpg/220px-Jingangjing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Jingangjing.jpg/330px-Jingangjing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Jingangjing.jpg/440px-Jingangjing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jingangjing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Sutra" title="Diamond Sutra">Diamond Sutra</a>, printed in 868, is the world's first widely printed book to include a specific date of printing.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">Woodblock printing</a> made the written word available to vastly greater audiences. One of the world's oldest surviving printed documents is a miniature Buddhist <i><a href="/wiki/Dharani" title="Dharani">dharani</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Sutra" title="Sutra">sutra</a> unearthed at Xi'an in 1974 and dated roughly from 650 to 670.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPan1997979–980_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPan1997979–980-322">[319]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Diamond_Sutra" title="Diamond Sutra">Diamond Sutra</a></i> is the first full-length book printed at regular size, complete with illustrations embedded with the text and dated precisely to 868.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETemple1986112_323-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETemple1986112-323">[320]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d151_324-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d151-324">[321]</a></sup> Among the earliest documents to be printed were Buddhist texts as well as calendars, the latter essential for calculating and marking which days were auspicious and which days were not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999124–125_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999124–125-325">[322]</a></sup> With so many books coming into circulation for the general public, literacy rates could improve, along with the lower classes being able to obtain cheaper sources of study. Therefore, there were more lower-class people seen entering the Imperial Examinations and passing them by the later Song dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006159-105">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200694_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman200694-326">[323]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999147_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999147-327">[324]</a></sup> Although the later <a href="/wiki/Bi_Sheng" title="Bi Sheng">Bi Sheng</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a> in the 11th century was innovative for his period, woodblock printing that became widespread in the Tang would remain the dominant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_typography_in_East_Asia" title="History of typography in East Asia">printing type in China</a> until the more advanced <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> from Europe became widely accepted and used in East Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d227_328-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d227-328">[325]</a></sup> The first use of the <a href="/wiki/Playing_card" title="Playing card">playing card</a> during the Tang dynasty was an auxiliary invention of the new age of printing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d131–132_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d131–132-329">[326]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cartography">Cartography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Cartography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dunhuang_star_map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="2128" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Dunhuang_star_map.jpg/220px-Dunhuang_star_map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Dunhuang_star_map.jpg/330px-Dunhuang_star_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Dunhuang_star_map.jpg/440px-Dunhuang_star_map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dunhuang_star_map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dunhuang_map" title="Dunhuang map">Dunhuang map</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_map" title="Star map">star map</a> showing the North Polar region. c. 700.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEXi1981464_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEXi1981464-330">[327]</a></sup> The whole set of star maps contains over 1,300 stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[328]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In the realm of <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a>, there were further advances beyond the map-makers of the Han dynasty. When the Tang chancellor <a href="/wiki/Pei_Ju" title="Pei Ju">Pei Ju</a> (547–627) was working for the Sui dynasty as a Commercial Commissioner in 605, he created a well-known gridded map with a <a href="/wiki/Scale_(map)" title="Scale (map)">graduated scale</a> in the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Pei_Xiu" title="Pei Xiu">Pei Xiu</a> (224–271).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a538–540,_543_332-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a538–540,_543-332">[329]</a></sup> The Tang chancellor <a href="/wiki/Xu_Jingzong" title="Xu Jingzong">Xu Jingzong</a> (592–672) was also known for his map of China drawn in the year 658.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a543_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a543-333">[330]</a></sup> In the year 785 the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Dezong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Dezong of Tang">Emperor Dezong</a> had the geographer and cartographer <a href="/wiki/Jia_Dan" title="Jia Dan">Jia Dan</a> (730–805) complete a map of China and her former colonies in Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a543_333-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a543-333">[330]</a></sup> Upon its completion in 801, the map was 9.1 m (30 ft) in length and 10 m (33 ft) in height, mapped out on a grid scale of one inch equaling one hundred <i><a href="/wiki/Li_(unit)" title="Li (unit)">li</a></i> (Chinese unit of measuring distance).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a543_333-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a543-333">[330]</a></sup> A Chinese map of 1137 is similar in complexity to the one made by Jia Dan, carved on a stone stele with a grid scale of 100 li.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986aPlate_LXXXI_334-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986aPlate_LXXXI-334">[331]</a></sup> However, the only type of map that has survived from the Tang period are <a href="/wiki/Star_chart" title="Star chart">star charts</a>. Despite this, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_cartography" title="Chinese cartography">the earliest extant terrain maps of China</a> come from the ancient <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">State of Qin</a>; maps from the 4th century BC that were excavated in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHsu199390_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHsu199390-335">[332]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine">Medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Chinese of the Tang era were also very interested in the benefits of officially classifying all of the <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">medicines</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Pharmacology" title="Pharmacology">pharmacology</a>. In 657, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Gaozong of Tang">Emperor Gaozong of Tang</a> (r. 649–683) commissioned the literary project of publishing an official <i><a href="/wiki/Materia_medica" title="Materia medica">materia medica</a></i>, complete with text and illustrated drawings for 833 different medicinal substances taken from different stones, minerals, metals, <a href="/wiki/History_of_opium_in_China" title="History of opium in China">plants</a>, herbs, animals, vegetables, fruits, and cereal crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenn2002235_336-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn2002235-336">[333]</a></sup> In addition to compiling pharmacopeias, the Tang fostered learning in medicine by upholding imperial medical colleges, state examinations for doctors, and publishing forensic manuals for physicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200483_307-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200483-307">[304]</a></sup> Authors of medicine in the Tang include Zhen Chuan (d. 643) and <a href="/wiki/Sun_Simiao" title="Sun Simiao">Sun Simiao</a> (581–682), the former who first identified in writing that patients with <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a> had an excess of sugar in their <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a>, and the latter who was the first to recognize that diabetic patients should avoid consuming alcohol and <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starchy</a> foods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETemple1986132–133_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETemple1986132–133-337">[334]</a></sup> As written by Zhen Chuan and others in the Tang, the <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">thyroid</a> glands of sheep and pigs were successfully used to treat <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goiter" title="Goiter">goiters</a>; thyroid extracts were not used to treat patients with goiter in the West until 1890.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETemple1986134–135_338-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETemple1986134–135-338">[335]</a></sup> The use of the <a href="/wiki/Amalgam_(dentistry)" title="Amalgam (dentistry)">dental amalgam</a>, manufactured from tin and silver, was first introduced in the medical text <i>Xinxiu Bencao</i> written by Su Gong in 659.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-czarnetzki_339-0"><a href="#cite_note-czarnetzki-339">[336]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Alchemy.2C_gas_cylinders.2C_and_air_conditioning"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Alchemy,_gas_cylinders,_and_air_conditioning">Alchemy, gas cylinders, and air conditioning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Alchemy, gas cylinders, and air conditioning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chinese scientists of the Tang period employed complex chemical formulas for an array of different purposes, often found through experiments of <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a>. These included a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterproof" title="Waterproof">waterproof</a> and dust-repelling cream or <a href="/wiki/Varnish" title="Varnish">varnish</a> for clothes and weapons, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fireproof" title="Fireproof">fireproof</a> cement for glass and porcelain wares, a waterproof cream applied to silk clothes of <a href="/wiki/Underwater_diving" title="Underwater diving">underwater divers</a>, a cream designated for polishing bronze mirrors, and many other useful formulas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e452_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986e452-340">[337]</a></sup> The vitrified, translucent ceramic known as <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a> was invented in China during the Tang, although many types of glazed ceramics preceded it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdshead200480_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdshead200480-217">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood199949_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood199949-341">[338]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ever since the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> (202 BC – 220 AD), the Chinese had drilled deep <a href="/wiki/Borehole" title="Borehole">boreholes</a> to transport natural gas from <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">bamboo pipelines</a> to stoves where <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> evaporation pans boiled <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> to extract salt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETemple198678–79_342-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETemple198678–79-342">[339]</a></sup> During the Tang dynasty, a <a href="/wiki/Gazetteer" title="Gazetteer">gazetteer</a> of Sichuan province stated that at one of these 182 m (600 ft) 'fire wells', men collected natural gas into portable bamboo tubes which could be carried around for dozens of km (mi) and still produce a flame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETemple198679–80_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETemple198679–80-343">[340]</a></sup> These were essentially the first <a href="/wiki/Gas_cylinder" title="Gas cylinder">gas cylinders</a>; Robert Temple assumes <a href="/wiki/Tap_(valve)" title="Tap (valve)">some sort of tap</a> was used for this device.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETemple198679–80_343-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETemple198679–80-343">[340]</a></sup>
</p><p>The inventor <a href="/wiki/Ding_Huan" title="Ding Huan">Ding Huan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Floruit" title="Floruit">fl.</a> 180 AD) of the Han dynasty invented a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fan_(mechanical)" title="Fan (mechanical)">rotary fan</a> for <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a>, with seven wheels 3 m (10 ft) in diameter and manually powered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b99,_151,_233_344-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b99,_151,_233-344">[341]</a></sup> In 747, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuanzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xuanzong of Tang">Emperor Xuanzong</a> had a "Cool Hall" built in the imperial palace, which the <i><a href="/wiki/Tang_Yulin" title="Tang Yulin">Tang Yulin</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">唐語林</span></span>) describes as having water-powered fan wheels for air conditioning as well as rising jet streams of water from fountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b134,_151_345-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b134,_151-345">[342]</a></sup> During the subsequent Song dynasty, written sources mentioned the air conditioning rotary fan as even more widely used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b151_346-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b151-346">[343]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_historiography" title="Chinese historiography">Chinese historiography</a></div>
<p>The first classic work about the Tang is the <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Book_of_Tang" title="Old Book of Tang">Old Book of Tang</a></i> by Liu Xu (887–946) et al. of the <a href="/wiki/Later_Jin_(Five_Dynasties)" title="Later Jin (Five Dynasties)">Later Jin</a>, who redacted it during the last years of his life. This was edited into another history (labeled the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Book_of_Tang" title="New Book of Tang">New Book of Tang</a></i>) in order to distinguish it, which was a work by the Song historians <a href="/wiki/Ouyang_Xiu" title="Ouyang Xiu">Ouyang Xiu</a> (1007–1072), Song Qi (998–1061), et al. of the Song dynasty (between the years 1044 and 1060). Both of them were based upon earlier annals, yet those are now lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[344]</a></sup> Both of them also rank among the <i><a href="/wiki/Twenty-Four_Histories" title="Twenty-Four Histories">Twenty-Four Histories</a></i> of China. One of the surviving sources of the <i>Old Book of Tang</i>, primarily covering up to 756, is the <i><a href="/wiki/Tongdian" title="Tongdian">Tongdian</a></i>, which <a href="/wiki/Du_You" title="Du You">Du You</a> presented to the emperor in 801. The Tang period was again placed into the enormous <a href="/wiki/Universal_history" title="Universal history">universal history</a> text of the <i><a href="/wiki/Zizhi_Tongjian" title="Zizhi Tongjian">Zizhi Tongjian</a></i>, edited, compiled, and completed in 1084 by a team of scholars under the Song dynasty Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Sima_Guang" title="Sima Guang">Sima Guang</a> (1019–1086). This historical text, written with three million <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a> in 294 volumes, covered the history of China from the beginning of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warring_States" title="Warring States">Warring States</a> (403 BC) until the beginning of the Song dynasty (960).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tang_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The polite form <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Dà Táng</i></span> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">大唐</span></span>, <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;"> </span>'Great Tang') was often used, such as in the names of books of the period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson20136_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson20136-4">[4]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">During the rule of the Tang the world population grew from about 190 million to approximately 240 million, a difference of 50 million. See also <a href="/wiki/Medieval_demography" title="Medieval demography">medieval demography</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-179"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-179">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Fordham_University" title="Fordham University">Fordham University</a> (2000) <a class="external text" href="http://www.fordham.edu/halsall/eastasia/romchin1.html" rel="nofollow">offers</a> <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hirth" title="Friedrich Hirth">Friedrich Hirth</a>'s (1885) translated passage from the <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Book_of_Tang" title="Old Book of Tang">Old Book of Tang</a></i>: "The emperor <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Yang_of_Sui" title="Emperor Yang of Sui">Yang-ti</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a> [605–617 C.E.] always wished to open intercourse with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Fu-lin</a>, but did not succeed. In the 17th year of the period Cheng-kuan [643 C.E.], the king of Fu-lin Po-to-li [<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constans_II_Pogonatus" title="Constans II Pogonatus">Constans II Pogonatus</a>, Emperor 641–668 C.E.] sent an embassy offering red glass, lu-chin-ching [green gold gems], and other articles. <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">T'ai-tsung</a> [the then ruling emperor] favored them with a message under his imperial seal and graciously granted presents of silk. Since the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Ta-shih</a> [the Arabs] had conquered these countries they sent their commander-in-chief, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">Mo-i (Mo'awiya)</a>, to besiege their capital city; by means of an agreement they obtained friendly relations, and asked to be allowed to pay every year tribute of gold and silk; in the sequel they became subject to Ta-shih. In the second year of the period Ch'ien-feng [667 C.E.] they sent an embassy offering Ti-yeh-ka. In the first year of the period Ta-tsu [701 C.E.] they again sent an embassy to our court. In the first month of the seventh year of the period K'ai-yuan [719 C.E.] their lord sent the ta-shou-ling [an officer of high rank] of T'u-huo-lo [Khazarstan] to offer lions and ling-yang[antelopes], two of each. A few months after, he further sent ta-te-seng ["priests of great virtue"] to our court with tribute."</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFWechsler1979">Wechsler, Howard J. (1979). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=idu6-Ie1MhwC&amp;pg=PA151" rel="nofollow"><i>Sui and T'ang China, 589–906. Part 1</i></a>. Cambridge, England: Cambridge University Press. p. 151. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1139055949" title="Special:BookSources/978-1139055949"><bdi>978-1139055949</bdi></a>. <q>This genealogy claimed by the T'ang royal house established its claim to be descended from a notable Han clan and to be members of a prominent north-western lineage. However, there is some reason to believe that this line of descent, presented as solid fact by the T'ang histories, was in fact a deliberate fabrication.<span class="nowrap"> </span>... Two of the men who, it has been suggested, were among the ancestors of <a href="/wiki/Li_Bing_(Northern_Zhou)" title="Li Bing (Northern Zhou)">Li Hu</a> [grandfather of Li Yuan] were the generals <a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E6%9D%8E%E5%88%9D%E5%8F%A4%E6%8B%94" title="zh:李初古拔">Li Ch'u-ku-pa</a> and Li Mai-te, whose names show that they had either adopted or been granted the Chinese surname Li, but retained alien, perhaps <a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Hsien-pei</a>, personal names.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Sui+and+T%27ang+China%2C+589%E2%80%93906.+Part+1&amp;rft.place=Cambridge%2C+England&amp;rft.pages=151&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1979&amp;rft.isbn=978-1139055949&amp;rft.aulast=Wechsler&amp;rft.aufirst=Howard+J.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Didu6-Ie1MhwC%26pg%3DPA151&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATang+dynasty"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFChen2012">Chen, Sanping (2012). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=ugbWH-5OjegC&amp;pg=PA5" rel="nofollow"><i>Multicultural China in the Early Middle Ages</i></a>. University of Pennsylvania Press. pp. 4–6. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8122-0628-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8122-0628-9"><bdi>978-0-8122-0628-9</bdi></a>. <q>More intriguingly, faced with the opposition of imperial court judges who wanted to uphold the mandatory capital punishment, Emperor Taizong explained that Falin's defamation of the royal ancestry "was not without foundation." Emperor Taizong apparently recognized that the imperial clan's genealogical connections to the Tuoba nobles and other "Barbarian" families were open contemporary knowledge<span class="nowrap"> </span>... The newest proof is the recent archaeological discovery that shows that another prominent Li clan of the period, namely that of <a href="/wiki/Li_Xian_(Northern_Zhou_general)" title="Li Xian (Northern Zhou general)">Li Xian</a>, a general in chief of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Zhou" title="Northern Zhou">Northern Zhou</a>, with the same claim to the Longxi ancestry, was in fact of unmistakable Tuoba Xianbei descent.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Multicultural+China+in+the+Early+Middle+Ages&amp;rft.pages=4-6&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Pennsylvania+Press&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8122-0628-9&amp;rft.aulast=Chen&amp;rft.aufirst=Sanping&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DugbWH-5OjegC%26pg%3DPA5&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATang+dynasty"></span></span>
</li>
